[Column 1]
UTAH STATE PEACE POEM.
	[Written by Josephine Spencer of the Deseret
News staff at request of the members of the
National Committee, and recited at the peace
demonstrations on May 15.]
We lift our voices in a note grown strong
	With gathering cadence of a world-sung chord,
To hush the ancient dissonance of wrong,
	And drown it in the music of the Word
	Whose lulled sound the Syrian shepherds heard
Haunting the spaces on that star-choired morn,
When heaven’s new covenant with earth was
born.

Peace the first mission of the sphere-sung King,
	Peace the sole message of the chanting stars
That Pleiades loosed from her happy ring
	To light his pathway to earth’s prison bars.
	Yet since that time the crimson light of Mars
Has dimmed its whiter flame with earth’s own
voice.
Yielding the willing dictum of her choice.

So long its pleading, like an echo swung,
	Vainly among the chambers of lone hills,
Down the worn highways of the world has rung
	To harass heedless ears and idle wills,
	That hardly now its rising clamor stills
The old discordance of the war-throned hate,
Vaunting itself a dynasty of Fate.

Yet strong and clearer than a lark’s high note
	That herald morning’s dominant young light.
The message, taken into myriad throats,
	Chants of the nearing conquest of the night;
	Already robed and charioted for flight,
War sits. and watches for the protocol
Soon to be written on the nation’s scroll.

Only the ambush of a weakened will,
	The parley of a traitor faith may slay
The hope that brightens on the cloud-crowned hill
	Where yet War’s all but vanquished legions
	stay,
	No other night than this may give the day
Long vainly battled, to Wars straggling hosts
And yield God’s banner still to Evil's boasts.

So many crying causes need our aid—
	Bereavement’s murmur, long without reply,
And all the bitter wrack the curse has laid
	Wherever earth’s stained pastures face the sky,
	Shame lays its cloud on hands that idle lie
Letting the blight drag out its hated dole
To darken coming ages as they roll—

Eyes that look on, while Science, hand in hand
	With Art, and all her noble sisterhood
Join to build up a second Eden, planned
	By Progress, working towards the eternal good;
	To see its great deliverance withstood
By this one laggard, jealous of the tears
Yielded as earth's sore tribute through the years.

[Column 2]
Yet ours to say if still the alien state
	Of the dark monarch burden coming time,
While other evils crumble, and the date
	Of each year’s cycle builds into the rhyme
	Of Right and Beauty. Nurtured in the slime
Of greed and hate, shall still this despot fight—
A recreant tool against the hosts of light?

No giant lists like this has earth yet seen
	Brought to the crisis of a last affray;
Not Marathon or Leuctra, or the green
	Young fields of freedom in the newer day
	Vaunted a cause whose widening, heaven-led
	way
Promised this peerless guerdon for their grace—
To lift War’s shackles from the human race.

And we shall hold that guerdon—not as one
	Gained hardly, through a lengthening span of
	years—
But quickly, as a thing now nearly done;
	Even now we sight its place. Soon, too, the
	cheers
	Of many peoples watching through their tears,
Shall rise, and men loud hailing Earth’s release—
Shall tune their life-strings to the Psalm of Peace.

[There is a photo of 6 women, 3 seated in the front row and 3 standing directly behind them in the second (and final) row.]

[The following is below the photograph.]
Florence Ashton	Geneve Clark	Florence Lloyd
Augusta Bylund	Lillie Shipp	Hazel Jones

	Our new departure in this first number of the new volume is giving our readers
the pictures of some of the girl students of the Latter-day Saints University in Salt
Lake City. One sees at a glance in their faces innocence, truthfulness and a high
order of intelligence. It is not given to any of us to read the future, but we are safe
in predicting for these young girls a fulness of development physical, moral, spir-
itual and intellectual as the years go by, if the best use is made of the opportunities now
opening before them. Beautiful in its unfolding is the life of the innocent maiden and
contemplating the subject one recalls to mind Longfellow’s words:

[The following is written in two columns beside each other, beneath the above pargraph.]
[Column 2.1]
“How beautiful is youth! how bright it gleams,
With its illusions, aspirations, dreams!
Book of Beginnings, story without End,
Each maid a heroine and each man a friend!
Aladdin's Lamp and Fortunatus’ Purse
That hold the treasures of the universe!

[Column 2.2]
"All possibilities are in its hand,
No danger daunts it. and no foe withstands;
In its sublime audacity of faith,
‘Be thou removed!’ it to the mountain saith,
And with ambitious feet secure and proud,
Ascends the ladder leaning on the cloud!"

THE MASTER’S STROKE. 

Touch Thou with gentle strokes divine, oh Lord!
Discordant tendrils of life’s harpsichord;
O, let the vibrant echoes thus awoke
Within men’s souls, enobling thought evoke. 
Let sweetest accents from the worlds unknown,
Change solemn moan to gentle patient tone.  
Sublime, triumphal anthems fill the air, 
To lift all human hearts from mirage of despair.

Let every throbbing pulse in nature’s tune
Accord with thine for aye. Oh very soon,
Dear Lord, may waves of music from above
Inspire in mortal lives unselfish love,
Till through the universe from shore to shore 
No discords will be heard forevermore!
Till as themselves all love their fellow men— 
The worlds all sing in praise the grand amen.
						ELLIS R. SHIPP.

[Column 1]
R. S. REPORT.
SALT LAKE STAKE.
	The quarterly conference of the Salt Lake-
Stake Relief Society was held on June 7,
1902, in the Assembly Hall. Meeting
commenced at 10 o’clock. President M.
I. Horne presiding.
	President Bathsheba W. Smith of the
General Society was present.
	Singing, “Do what is right.” Prayer
by Sister Mary A. Lambert. Singing,
“We thank Thee, O God, for a prophet.”
Roll called and minutes of last conference
read and accepted.
	President Horne said it seemed there
was a lack of interest in our conferences,
we should not let our worldly cares detain
us from our duties. Noticed that worldly
celebrations will bring out many sisters,
felt our duties should be first and pleasure
secondary. Spoke of the duties of the
sisters as Relief Society members and
daughters of our Father in heaven.
	President B W. Smith said remember to
the faithful the promises are made. Be-
lieved if we would pay our tithes and
offerings, keep the Sabbath day, etc.,
we would be more interested in our con-
ferences and other meetings.
	Sister Ruth M. Fox felt as she had
heard it expressed, that it takes all faith
and diligence and constant watching to
keep us in the line of our duty, felt thank-
ful she was worthy to be numbered with
these women here. Believed though we
do not meet the same trials that our parents
and older members of the Church had, we
will all be tested.
	Sister Maria Y. Dougall, counselor to
President E. S. Taylor, knew the Lord
would bless us in every effort we put forth
to build up his kingdom. Spoke of our
faithfulness in our first estate and the
privilege we now enjoy because of it. Said
not to ask honor of men, be humble and
work out our own salvation. We must
find the virtues of others; we must seek to
build others up if we wish to build our-
selves up. The Lord must have our co-
operation to bear off this kingdom.
	Sister Lydia D. Alder, of the Salt Lake
Stake Board, spoke of the good example of
leading sisters of the Relief Society. Spoke
of the importance of the teachers in visit-
ing their blocks and in administering to
the needs of the people, relieving the dis-
tressed. Spoke of the spreading of the
Gospel truths. Said our aim was for an
exaltation, while those not of our faith are
merely seeking a salvation, we are respon-
sible for the way we live the Gospel. Our
acts with our children, in the home, before
our sisters, etc., are great responsibilities.
	Sister Margaret C. Hull sang a solo,
“The End of the Way,” which was very
much enjoyed.
	President Horne spoke of the work re-
quired of us to gain an exaltation, also of the
sin of David. Our Father will deal justly
with us even though He is merciful. We
must pass through this second estate faith-
fully.
	Apostle J. H. Smith said mother
was the dearest word in the English lan-
guage, believed the woman is more willing
to bear the curse laid upon her than the
man. Men are not willing to be farmers
and producers but are seeking other em-
ployment. Manual labor is looked down
upon, but that is wrong.
	Meeting adjourned until 2 o’clock.

[Column 2]
Singing, “Our God we raise to Thee.”
Benediction by President Sarah Jane
Cannon.
	Afternoon session, opened at 2 o’clock.
Singing, “Lord we come before Thee
now.” Prayer by Brother C. J. Thomas.
Singing, hymn, by the Temple sextette.
	Sister Sarah J. Cannon, counselor in the
Salt Lake Stake of Zion, desired with all
her heart and soul to do her work in the
Relief Society. She prayed all might have
the spirit of the Relief Society work, she
knew that was necessary in Temple work
and it must be in the Relief Society. We
want to do all that we should. Our first
duty is to the poor, to take care of them,
we understand that, and there are many
other things required of us. We must be
diligent if we would obtain the promised
blessings. Made other very interesting
and instructive remarks.
	Counselor Clara C. Cannon said there
are so many things mothers should know;
they should gather the children and
teach them the principles of the Gospel, it
will tend to make our children waver if we
do not live lives of Latter-day Saints.
“My mother was a consistent Latter-day
Saint, she it was who made the deep im-
pression upon me.”
	Sister E. B Wells felt we ought, in the
Centre Stake, to have a house full. Bore
testimony of the divinity of the Gospel and
the mission of the Prophet Joseph Smith.
“Very few understand the magnitude of the
Relief Society, much that is done cannot
be recorded. There are fifty Stakes and
about seven hundred branches, the average
attendance is very small. In far-off New
Zealand the attendance is almost the entire
enrollment. We have been given per-
mission to have mother’s classes in the
Relief Society, but the chairman of the
mother’s meeting is chosen by and subject
to the president of the respective society.
It is like the teacher of a school being
under the principal of the school.” Made
other suggestive remarks.
	Apostle J. Henry Smith said the work
of the Relief Society has been very great.
You can tell the character of a community
by a very brief visit among the women of
that community. If the ladies are not inter-
ested in all that makes progression, there
is a lack of thrift, both in the home and in
the community. In the homes where the
mothers are religiously inclined, the chil-
dren have been trained in the spirit, and
mothers have sought to impress upon them
the importance of industrial labor and other
virtues. The forceful power a mother has
upon the child will be plainly visible. The
president of Mexico had compared to him
the difference of the advantages of the
United States to that of his people, who
had been bound down by their conquerors.
He said: “Sisters, your education has been
to regenerate mankind. If you heard it in
foreign lands, you were convinced the Al-
mighty had spoken from the heavens and
you must have His blessing. Many of
you have separated from loved ones, every
effort was made to secure that which
would bring you to that which you know
to be all worth having. You have shown
your heroic character, laid the foundation
of home and invited others to partake of
the same privileges. The most successful
ones have been the girls, those who thro’
faith in God gathered to this remote
region. You have been schooled to
battle with the elements of nature”

[Column 3]
Spoke of the early days when the wife was
often deprived of her husband for the
numerous public duties and obligations,
mothers of their sons for the same cause.
Many have performed their duty to their
country with patriotic devotion, incom-
parable, impossible to tell how the women
have nobly done their part. “Sisters, I
have nothing but commendation and
blessings for you. Your sons have also
been defending their country in Cuba and
the Phillipines, no body of women have
been more heroic. You have contributed
to build this Temple for the redemption of
the dead and have been diligent in good
works from the beginning.
	Miss Owen sang a solo, “Beyond the
Gates of Paradise.”
	President John R. Winder felt that the
prophet made no mistake when he
organized the Relief Society. It has mul-
tiplied and increased, it was the first
auxiliary organization of the Church. “You
have accomplished a great deal of good and
are better prepared now than ever before
to go forward and do more. God bless
you; may your substance be multiplied
and your lives be prolonged until you are
satisfied.” Rejoiced in the great work.
	Sister E. J. Stevenson spoke of the
women attending their meetings with their
babies in their arms as they do in the
outstanding Stakes. Felt they were noble
women to bear the spirits reserved to come
forth. How necessary to teach our daugh-
ters to be noble mothers.
	President Horne said the needs of society
work is not only to relieve the poor but to 
save souls, to increase faith in one another.
Cautioned the sisters to take good care of
the wheat and add to it this year. Bishop
Preston said to advise the sisters who had
no granaries to send the money they have
for wheat to Richfield, where they have a
very large elevator. Made closing re-
marks and adjourned conference for three
months.
	The Temple choir sang, “Praise to God
immortal praise.” Benediction by Apostle
J. H. Smith.
			J. S. WOOLLEY, Sec.


A FAITHFUL SISTER GONE.
	Sarah Newton Jones, born December 20, 1840,
at Hale Forting Bridge, England, died May 10,
1902, at Union, Salt Lake Co. She was second
vice-president in our Relief Society.
	A faithful woman has left us, a wise counselor,
an active worker and an efficient teacher for a
number of years. An embodiment of all that was
good and virtuous, she never was blest with chil-
dren of her own, but was a noble woman and a
true mother to her husband’s family.
	She embraced the Gospel with her father’s
family in her girlhood, but was the only one of
her family that came to Utah and gathered with
the Saints. She crossed the plains in Captain
Warren Snow’s company and arrived in Salt Lake
City October 4, 1864. She was married to Brother
Daniel Jones the same year.
	She was taken sick at the East Jordan Stake
conference May 4. Her sickness was of short
duration but very severe. All that loving hands
could do for her was done by her husband and his
family, who mourn her loss. She was cheerful to
the last and we know of no one more fitted for the
change that awaits the faithful.
	The funeral took place on May 12. There were a
number of speakers from different parts of the
Stake who testified of her love and integrity for
the Gospel. We mourn her absence but rejoice in
her rest, for we know she will receive the reward
of the faithful.
		M. A SMART, Pres.
		MARY ANDERSON, 1st Vice-Pres.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
THE FLAG NOT PROPERLY PROTECTED.
	Notwithstanding the fact that the Star
Spangled Banner is the oldest flag of all the
civilized powers, there is no national law.
to protect it from desecration. This flag of
the thirteen colonies of North America was
adopted when at war with the mother
country in 1777. The yellow and red flag
of Spain came out in 1785: the French tri-
color in 1794; The red English emblem,
with the present Union Jack, dates from
1801; the Italian flag was first unfurled in
1848; the present German in 1871; and the
Russian tricolor is quite a recent affair.
	These countries have laws to punish
desecration of their national flag: 
	Italy.—Law of June 30, 1889, Articles
15, 115: “Whoever with intention of con-
tempt should take same, destroy or insult
in a public place, or in a place open to the
public, the flag or any other emblem of the
state, will be punished by detention from
three to twenty months.”
	Austria-Hungary.—The Hungarian
criminal law 50, 1879, paragraph 37, says:
“No more than two months’ arrest and
not more than three hundred florins fine
should be inflicted upon any one....
who, with intent to insult, defiles, damages
or tears down the flag or coat of arms
publicly, used as the insignia of either
civil or military authority.”

[Column 2]
	Germany.—Articles 135, 303, inflict a
fine up to three hundred thalers or punished
with imprisonment up to two years.
	Greece.—Act 141 of the penal code pro-
vides imprisonment and prosecution by the
district attorney of the place in which the
outrage of the flag is committed, with in-
carceration for three months maximum.
	Russia.—Penalty is two to nineteen
years in jail.
	Brazil.—Article 100 of penal code, for
desecration of national colors, has a
penalty of imprisonment for not less than
six months to one year. Decree No. 3,346
of the fourteenth of October, 1887,
places the penalty of insult to the flag, one
hundred to five hundred dollars.
	Mexico.—Has a legal provision against
the desecration of or insult to the national
colors.
	China.—Punishment under one of the
laws relating to the desecration of
insignia.
	Spain.—The penal code provides a penal-
ty for insult to or desecration of the nation-
al flag.
	Portugal.—Penal code, Article 424,181,
six months to one year’s imprison-
ment.
	France.—Desecration of the national

[Column 3]
flag punishable under Sections 257, 379 and 
75 of penal code. 
	Great Britain.—Outrage to the national
colors can be dealt with under the laws
dealing with sedition or treason.
	Chili, Peru, Bulgaria, Roumania, Bel-
gium, and all other foreign governments
which have no special legal provisions can
“bring an action before either the civil or
military tribunal for any injudicious use of
its national flag under a general provision
of the code.”
	In the United States there are state
laws, New York has one, so has Illinois
and Massachusetts.
	Several flag bills have been introduced
in the United States Congress, the first by
the late S. S. Cox in the seventies;
Twelve years later another bill passed the
House, but was rejected by the Senate.
Representative W. E. Barrett, of Massa-
chusetts, presented a bill and a petition
with 100,000 signatures, of no avail.
There is an American Flag Association,
Col. Ralph Waldo Prime, of New York,
president. Solidly organized, with com-
mittees of thirteen members each, repre-
senting various patriotic societies, it is
hoped that sufficient pressure will be
brought to bear to win national legislation.
	State legislation has been sought, eleven
falling into line.—Patriotic Review.

Laura Young		Jennie Taylor		Naomi Unger	Lydia Weston	Lizzie Emery		Martha Wilkinson	Afton Rampton

	This group of faces of the girl students at the L. D. S. University speaks volumes of itself to those who are care-
ful observers of the fruits of the Gospel. In this picture there are possibly, and indeed probably those who will be
teachers of the youth of Zion and the future mothers of Prophets and Apostles. Something of the destiny of Zion
lies with them. There is great need of strength of character and of superior ability, and these young students look
as if they might possess both. Let us hope that some of them will be writers, poets perhaps, and that they will
distinguish themselves in art, science and religion, above all true religion. The Savior said, “Where much is given
much is required,” and great things will be expected of the women of the future, and assuredly our girls
will be endowed with those virtues that are the peculiar adornment of true and refined womanhood.

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, JUNE, 1 & 15, 1902.


NOTICE.
	A booklet has been issued by the Gener-
al Relief Society, to which the following
note has been added since its publication.
It is inserted in the “EXPONENT” for the
information of persons who received copies
of the pamphlet previous to the insertion of
this addition:
	Note to page 77.—It is due to Dr.
Margaret C. Roberts to add that while the
importance and benefits of the establish-
ment of classes for instruction in nursing
were thought of and talked about for many
years before they were organized in this
Stake of Zion, the practical work and con-
duct of these classes was first undertaken
by her, and she is largely entitled to the
credit of the success that has attended
them under the auspices of the Relief
Society.”
	All inquiries in reference to the Relief
Society Mother’s Classes should be directed
to Ida S. Dusenberry, Provo, and all cor-
repondence relating to the Nurse Class or
Nurse Work, should be addressed to Annie
T. Hyde, 40 West, North Temple Street,
Salt Lake City, Utah.
			BATHSHEBA W. SMITH,
			ANNIE T. HYDE,
			IDA SMOOT DUSENBERRY.


[Column 1]
THE BEST LIGHT AND KNOWLEDGE.
	At the commencement of the new
volume it was ardently desired to have
some new departure in the paper, that
might awaken a greater interest in its
patrons; but the simple fact of its publish-
ing the Conferences of the Relief Society
and making it a record of woman’s work
in all lands, ought to endear it to our
sisters, who are engaged in charitable and
philanthropic work, for the advancement
of truth and its establishment, and be-
cause it stands for right and righteousness.
	In these last days that are so pregnant
with evil tendencies and influences, and the
insidious foes that lurk on every side to
lead astray the innocent and unwary and
make them forgetful of God and His
promises to the Saints, all that makes for
better hopes and aspirations, purer motives
and correct living should be fostered and
maintained.
	Life is full of opportunities for doing
good to others and especially are the
Latter-day Saints blest in having so many
avenues open to them of imparting knowl-
edge and intelligence. It is to the Saints
that the Lord is revealing His will concern-

[Column 2]
ing the earth and its inhabitants, and the
Elders and missionaries who go forth to
the world have a message to convey that is
not a myth but a reality. Women as well
as men have part and lot in this matter;
even our young women, though compara
tively few, may be sent to the nations, yet
they have many rare opportunities at home
to impart information that is entirely differ-
ent to the views of the world at large.
	The lessons our young girls are taught
of faith, obedience and fidelity are a power-
ful incentive to a higher and better life, and
if believed in and practised must uplift the
standard of excellence.
	If our sisters only lived up to their
privileges what an influence they might
exert over their associates and those with
whom they are constantly brought in con-
tact.
	And the work for the redemption of the
dead, there is no limit to the extent of this
field of labor. Perhaps none of us realize
the magnitude of this magnificent ministra-
tion in the Temples erected in this dispen-
srtion; where even many of our young peo-
ple have the sacred privilege of doing vicar-
ious work for those long since departed,
who cannot act for themselves.
	To no other people have these things
been revealed, and because of this spirit of
Elijah that has come, that will turn the
hearts of the fathers to the children and of
the children to the fathers, other Temples
must be built and dedicated to the Lord
that this may be accomplished.
	One is apt to reach backward to the
ancient temples of Egypt and India, and
the student of history ponders over their
greatness and their surpassing grandeur
and durability, and is filled with admira-
tion and astonishment at the workmanship
of these ancient peoples. But greater
works are to be manifest and miracles sur-
passing those of stone and mortar in the
years that are fast approaching, and the
children of the Saints will necessarily be
the builders of greater and more far-famed
temples than those of which we are now so
justly proud. To do this great and mighty
work those who would take part therein
must be endowed from on high, but they
must be in and of themselves above all else
pure in heart to have part and portion in
this marvelous work.
	The Prophet Jeremiah said of this day:
“It shall no more be said the Lord liveth
who brought up the children of Israel out
of the land of Egypt.
	“But the Lord liveth that brought up
the children of Israel from the land of the
North, and from all the lands whither he
had driven them, and I will bring them
again into their land that I gave unto their
fathers.
	“Behold I will send for many fishers and
they shall fish them, and for many hunters
and they shall hunt them from every
mountain and from every hill and out of
the holes in the rocks."
	But in the meantime there must be
character building that will surpass in true
merit the highest intellectual and spiritual
attainment of the present generation.
Are we laying this foundation in the
schools, the colleges and the homes of the
Latter-day Saints of today? Do the peo
ple, do the mothers of the time consider
the advantages coming to them and the re-
sponsibility resting upon them because of
these higher avenues that are constantly
being opened up in the spread of knowl-

[Column 3]
edge and of greater light, that has been
vouchsafed through the goodness and
mercy of our Father in heaven? Assured-
ly not, and perhaps it is best that we can-
not see too much at once, for we must only
take a step at a time and be lead by the
Spirit into the greater light, which even
now is almost beyond our capacity to
comprehend.
	Some of us who have seen the small be-
ginnings in this land when it was but a
desert, can scarcely credit the growth of
the cities and towns and surroundings
even now in evidence; it seems almost in-
credible that coming here without means,
obliged to depend upon the efforts of our
own toil, the Latter-day Saints by the help
of the Lord have made the desert “blossom
as the rose,” have filled this land so invit-
ingly with orchards and vineyards and
fields of golden grain.
	Notwithstanding what has already been
accomplished the work increases and as the
march of progress goes forward there is
necessarily more to do to keep up with the
times. Our young sisters will need to be
on the alert, consistently active and ener-
getic in the pursuit of knowledge and of
wisdom, and with great advantages of edu-
cation such as are now enjoyed in all the
arts and sciences, including inventions and
languages, remember always that the Gos-
pel is first and obedience is the first prin-
ciple that will lead to the fount of all true
wisdom.


PRIMARY BANQUET.

	After the General Conference of the
Primary Associations, which was held in
the Assembly Hall in this city, May 22,
1902, the General officers of the society
gave a banquet to the visiting represen-
tatives from the several Stakes of Zion to
which the First Presidency and Patriarch
of the Church, with other friends of the
cause were invited.	The large gathering
was given in the commodious rooms of the
Sixteenth Ward hall which is admirably
adapted to such occasions. There were
four long tables set the length of the hall
loaded with the delicacies of the season and
tastefully decorated with flowers. At the
head of one of the tables sat President
Joseph F. Smith and some members of his
family, including President Bathsheba W.
Smith, of the General Relief Society.	At
the head of another table near was Presi-
dent John R. Winder and wife and other
celebrities, and at another still was Sister
Aurelia S. Rogers, the originator of the
Primary Association movement. Presi-
dent Joseph F. Smith pronounced the
blessing. There were possibly from four
to five hundred guests assembled.
	Sister Aurelia S. Rogers made a brief
address in a very modest way on the
thought, development and outcome of the
Primary as a help in Zion. There were
songs and music and some dancing by the
little ones after the feasting was over.
Little Miss Williams sang “The Holy
City” in sweet, tender tones that thrilled
the audience.
	Later in the evening there was a trolley
ride through the city and extending to
Forest Dale, in which a large number of
the company participated.
	The whole affair was a perfect social
success and thoroughly enjoyable. Presi-
dent Louie Felt and her able corps of
assistants are to be congratulated, and de-


serve great praise for the happy thought
that brought together in such pleasant
fashion so many earnest workers, many of
them from remote localities.	The writer
extends thanks for the invitation which
gave her such a favorable opportunity of
meeting sisters from a distance, as well as
those very dear and honored ones of our
own city, whose busy lives hinder in a
great measure the social intercourse one
needs along life’s journey.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT			5


RECEPTION AT THE BEE HIVE.

	The Young Peoples’ Conference which
convened May 31 and June 1, 1902, was
preceded on the Wednesday evening pre-
vious by a reception tendered by the Gen-
eral Board to the representatives from all
parts of the Stakes of Zion or other locali-
ties.	The General officers of the Relief
Society were also honored with an invita-
tion, as were the Apostles, Presidents of
Seventies, Presidents of Stakes and other
brethren and sisters, several hundred in-
vitations being issued.	There was some
dancing outside on the lawn, and inside all
seemed royally happy.	President Joseph
F. Smith in a most genial and happy man-
ner acted as host to all the numerous
guests, and made every one feel the
genuineness of his hospitality and brotherly
love.
	During the evening there were a few
recitations given, some songs rendered
and good music furnished, and there were
also delicious refreshments served by a
bevy of interesting and attractive young
“Mutual” girls.
	The grand old historic house with its
spacious rooms is admirably suited to large
entertainments, and to those who have
known it in the palmy days of its original
owner, President Brigham Young, it re-
calls many interesting reminiscences that
stir one’s soul to its very depths, and yet,
when filled with the happy throngs of
eager and genuine Mutual Improvement
workers it brings vividly to view the pres-
ent and future of Zion’s development and
growth; and what must eventually be the
results of these earnest and devoted mem-
bers of this great and grand organization
on the growth of Zion: the strength of
Israel’s hosts in this goodly land. 	It is a
real vision one may well contemplate with
hopefulness and expectancy of the great
triumph of truth over error in the ultimate
achievements of the splendid work of
spreading the Gospel far and wide to all
lands and among all peoples. This bring-
ing into nearer social relations these bands
of young men and women will assuredly
give them insight into each others lives
that will be delightful in its after thought
and effects, and the benefits must certainly
be of a stimulating and inspirational char-
acter, entering into the home and social
surroundings of even those far off.

	EDITORIAL NOTES.

	Through the courtesy of  Prof. Paul of
the L. D. S. University we are able to give
two pictures of “our girls,” students in
that famous school.	The offer to allow us
to use them was a kindly and polite favor,
and the editor takes pleasure in acknowl-
edging the same with sincere thanks.

	The Relief Society pamphlets recently
issued by the General officers of the Society

[column 2]
are for sale at the office of the WOMAN'S
EXPONENT, and it is the wish of those
interested in the book that it should be in
the hands of all officers of the society as it
contains general instructions and other
matter for the sisters,that will promote the
growth and development of the society.
Price 25cts.
	The election of officers of the Utah
Society Daughters of the Revolution
occurred on Tuesday, June 10, 1902, at 3
p. m., in the office of the WOMAN'S
EXPONENT, State Regent Mrs. Clarissa S.
Williams in the chair. Officers selected for
the ensuing year are as follows:	Regent,
Mrs. Julia P. M. Farnsworth; First Vice-
Regent, Mrs Isabel M. W. Sears; Second
Vice-Regent, Mrs. Ella W. Hyde; Cor.
Sec’y, Mrs. Phebe Y. Beatie; Rec. Sec’y,
Mrs. Elizabeth R. Wright; Assistant Rec.
Sec’y, Clarice Y. Spencer; Treasurer, Mrs.
Myra Y. Rossiter; Registrar, Mrs Mary E.
R. Webber; Historian, Mrs. E. B. Wells;
Chaplain, Mrs. C S. Williams; Press Com-
mittee, Mrs. Claire C. Benedict and Mrs.
Julia F. Lund.
	The several Conferences so far visited by
the General Board and missionary sisters
since the notices sent out to each Stake on
the list of appointments, are: East Jordan,
Emery, Nebo, Juab, Beaver, Parowan,
Bear Lake, Tooele, Pocatello, North and
South Sanpete, Cassia, Union, Box Elder,
Malad and Sevier.	The sisters who have
gone to these Conferences are: Counselors
Annie T. Hyde and Ida Smoot Dusen-
berry, Sisters E. B. Wells, Secretary, and
Clarissa S. Williams, treasurer, of the
General officers, Sarah J. Cannon, Susan
Grant, Elizabeth J. Stevenson, Julia P. M.
Farnsworth and Phebe Y. Beatie, of the
General Board.	Assistants Margaret A.
Caine, L. L. Greene Richards and Lydia
D. Alder.
	Mrs. Susa Y. Gates, of Provo, who was
elected chairman of the Press Committee
of the National Council of the United
States last February has been given the
proxy ship of the International Council of
Women for its executive session this year
in Copenhagen, Denmark, Mrs. Ida
Husted Harper, the chairman, not being
able to attend herself.	This gives Mrs.
Gates a great opportunity to display her
powers as a writer and correspondent in
sending out matter to the various countries,
not only of Europe, but other lands	It
will bring her in contact with able writers
and newspaper people the world over al-
most, who will be in attendance at this im-
portant session.	This is the third session
of the International Council since the
Quinquennial in London in 1899. The
next Quinquennial will be held in Berlin,
Germany, in 1904.	There will be one
more executive session in 1903, the time
and place yet to be determined.


[Column 1]
GENERAL CONFERENCE RELIEF SOCIETY.

	The General Conference of the Relief
Society which was held on Monday, April
7, 1902, convened in the L. D. S. Hall in
Salt Lake City, at 10 a. m., President
Bathsheba W. Smith presiding.
	Conference opened by singing, "Come,
let us rejoice," etc An eloquent prayer
that touched every heart was offered by
Mrs. Clarissa S. Williams. Continued by
singing, "We thank Thee, O God, for a
prophet" The secretary, Mrs. E. B.

[Column 2]
Wells, called the roll of officers and General
Board, nearly every one present; also
called the presidents of Stakes, over forty
responding.
	President B. W. Smith greeted the con-
gregation with a hearty welcome. She
said: "I am happy to meet with you this
morning, glad to see so large a congrega-
tion assembled, feel we are engaged in a
glorious work. Being crowded together as
we are here in this room will perhaps make 
us feel more the need of a place of our own
and encourage us to bring our mites to-
gether for a building such as we have been
talking of for some time. I will say there
is to be a Memorial Hall to the Prophet
Joseph erected on this block and it will be
large enough to have offices for all the
General authorities of the Church, the
Relief Society will have their building
with this, as will the Young Ladies’
and Primary associations, and it will be
better for us than any other way. The
Prophet Joseph organized the Relief Society
and gave us many instructions how to
carry on our meetings and how to treat
each other. May the Lord bless us in this
conference.”
	All reports from Stakes were limited to
three minutes.
	Morgan Stake was represented by
Lydia Rich, who reported they were all
united and had visited twice a year regu-
larly. 
	Alberta, Canada reported by Sister 
Katharine Pilling who stated they were
trying to do the best they could, had
visited all around this year giving all the
encouragement possible and urged the 
people to attend to their duties.
	Benson Stake, President Rebecca Raw-
lins, the Stake has been recently organized
in November, 1901, had held one confer-
ence and done some visiting among the
sisters.
	Beaver by Sister Jane Bickley who gave
a good account of the work and spoke in
praise of Sister Shepherd, the president,
who had recently been very sick and was
not able to come to conference.
	Bingham, President Emma J. Bennett,
felt good in beholding the faces of so many
mothers and dear old sisters, and the pic-
tures of our prophets that were hung upon
the walls, thought we ought to feel happy
in such a gathering. In her Stake the
sisters were trying to help with means for
the building and also to save grain and to
magnify their calling by doing every duty.
	Box Elder, President Susannah P.
Booth was glad to meet with the sisters
and could make a good report for Box
Elder Stake, sisters were faithful and
energetic, had given liberally for the
building and were always ready to respond
to every call made upon them, were dili-
gent in attending to the sick and the
needy.
	Cache Stake reported by Sister Margaret
Merrill, treasurer, who gave an excellent
account of the condition of the sisters and .
of the labors and duties performed by
them.
	Emery Stake by Sister McKee, Hunting-
ton, who spoke of the reorganization, and
that very little time had elapsed since but
the wards were all in good condition and
the presiding sisters were prepared to go
ahead with the visiting around among the
people.
	Granite Stake, President Emma S.
Woodruff, “Our Stake is in good condition,


[Column 1]
our officers’ meetings are very beneficial,
we have the missionaries and we change
from one ward, to another to visit our sis-
ters, and we also have two appointed to
visit those living among us who are not of
our faith.”
	Jordan Stake represented by Counselor
H. H. Larson, who gave a most excellent
report of their financial condition and the
liberal contributions of the society to the
Woman’s Building, thought all were dili-
gent.
	Mrs. Leah Dunford Widtsoe then ad-
dressed the conference upon Domestic
Science, speaking first to the mothers and
grandmothers, of the wonderful experiences
and success in raising good children under
such adverse circumstances and helping to
make Zion beautiful and filling it with
happy homes. Referred to the daughters
who expect to marry and become mothers,
thought they should be taught in the
science of housekeeping and home making.
Said we now have the schools where
domestic science is taught and they will be
of great benefit in the community. Our
girls many of them never do anything in
their homes, she knew of girls who had a
fine education, could play the piano, but
could not even make a plain gingham
apron, and they marry and are not quali-
fied to perform the duties or superintend
the home. By and by, children are born
and cares increase and duties multiply and
they are not equipped for the struggles that
must be met. One of the first things
necessary is to know how to make good
bread. Mothers are sometimes at fault in
not teaching the girls because it is easier
to do it themselves, but it is the duty of
every girl to look into these domestic mat-
ters and prepare for home making and life
will be sweeter and better.
	Kanab Stake President, Artemissia Seeg-
miller said we have one ward in which we
have no Relief Society at present but we
expect to organize there soon, we are very
united and we are trying to live up to
every requirement; we recently had a visit
from Sister E. J. Stevenson. We very
rarely have our sisters from the city, as we
are remote from railroads and at some
times of the year it is bad traveling. We
are doing all we possibly can under the
circumstances in our locality.
	Malad Stake reported by Counselor
Mary Stewart who gave a good report
and spoke kindly of the president, Lucinda
Hoskins thought the sisters were diligent
and trying to do all in their power and
were deserving of great credit.
	Parowan Stake reported by Sister Lettie
Dailey, president of the Relief Society at
Summit. They had not held conference as
usual because of contagious disease, meet-
ings had to be discontinued, but knew all
were trying to do their best.
	St. Joseph Stake, Arizona, represented
by Sister Virginia Majors who had recently
come from that country. The president
and counselors were united and doing a
most excellent work, their labors were
highly appreciated, thought they were
striving to do all in their power.
	Teton Stake, President Sophia Rigby
said that Stake had only been organized
the 1st and 2nd of September, 1901, six
months, had held officers meetings month-
ly, were trying to do every duty they had
been counseled to do, and tried to be
energetic and aim for the best in all things.
	Wasatch Stake, President Annie R.
Duke. “We try and visit our Stake

[Column 2]
regularly, we advise the sisters to give to
the Building and to the dime fund.” Spoke
of being in Nauvoo when the society was
organized, though only a child.
	Union Stake, Oregon. President Mary
E. Schofield reported Union as a very new
Stake had recently added one more ward
making seven branches. “We are trying to
do our duty and are desirous of learning,
and to be in unison with other Stakes and
keep up with them. We are glad of this
opportunity of attending a General Con-
ference of the Relief Society.
	Pocatello Stake reported by Counselor
Matilda Smith who spoke of the great
work done in that city and of the desire
the sisters manifest to know what to do in
the way of improvement.
	Hyrum Stake, President Mary E. How-
ells said, “We have ten wards and we are
very new in the work, have only had one
conference, I am glad to be here to report,
we have donated liberally to the Building
and have paid our ten cent fund also.
	Counselor Ida Smoot Dusenberry made
some explanations about giving individual
receipts, these must be given in the ward to
which the one who donated belonged.
	The treasurer, Clarissa S. Williams,
made some further explanations about
giving receipts.
	First Counselor Annie T. Hyde gave
notice to the presidents of Stakes about the
conferences being visited and explained in
regard to the appointments sent out.
	The secretary read notices of other
meetings and spoke of the lunch which
was waiting in Richards Street.
	Morning session closed with singing
“The Spirit of God like a fire is burning,”
etc. Dismissed with prayer by President
M. Isabella Horne.
	Afternoon session, 2 p. m., at the
Assembly Hall, President B. W. Smith
presiding, opened by the Temple choir
singing, "Come, come ye Saints.” Prayer
by Sister Julina L. Smith. Singing,
“Redeemer of Israel.”
	President B. W. Smith was pleased to
see so many present and spoke a few mo-
ments on the necessity of the sisters keep-
ing the word of wisdom and of paying
their tithes and donations, and that they
would be blest in so doing.
	Apostle Reed Smoot, who had just come
into the hall, made an eloquent address,
thought it a privilege to speak to those
assembled; in looking over the audience
and observing those who had labored so
long and faithfully, he recognized their
devotion to the principles of the Gospel
and fidelity to the truth. The First Presi-
dency and Apostles, Presidents of Stakes
and all the authorities in the Church appre-
ciate the Relief Society, they know where
to turn when there is need of help. “We
younger people have to live about a life
time to find out who our friends are. We
find after our mother is gone that there is
no such friend as our mother was, and yet
there is wisdom perhaps even in that.
When I have been in the world and heard
the women of Utah spoken evil of I have
wished they could come and know and feel
the spirit of the work in which the sisters
are engaged.” Apostle Smoot urged the
sisters to teach their boys reverence for the
priesthood, have them ordained deacons
and move forward and they will go on and
become useful men. “God bless our girls,
for they will not marry those who will not
go into the House of the Lord. I know
the sacrifice our s[--]ters are willing to make.

[Column 3]
I know the sacrifices by my own mother,
how true and faithful she was in the midst
of trial, though I did not realize it then.
The whole burden of the conference just
past was that ‘Zion is growing.’ The
time is coming when womanhood will be
advanced. God is the Father of our spirits
and we will bring our children forth in
purity and prepare them to go back into
the presence of our Father in heaven. I
feel we are working to this end, I feel that
you are and have been working along these
lines. In regard to woman suffrage that
has been given to us, you who have worked
for it have helped to bring it to the climax
it has reached.” Apostle Smoot then
answered some questions in regard to
women presiding and commended the sis-
ters labors in the highest terms, and closed
by saying, “God bless you and peace be
with you and keep you safe, and may you
go forward increasing in numbers and all
is that good.”
	Mrs. Hal J. Moore sang “Jesus, my
Redeemer.”
	The secretary, Mrs. E. B. Wells, read
the annual report and made some remarks
concerning the energy of the Relief Society
sisters in New Zealand and the Sandwich
Islands. The report was approved as read.
	First Counselor Annie T. Hyde said in
looking over the congregation and think-
ing of the love that had been inspired that
could bring them together, she had thought
of Sister Eliza and Sister Smith, Sister
Richards and Sister Zina, whose words had
been like music in our ears all the time.
We have all thought and wondered what
we could do to make our meetings more
interesting to the young women. We
want lectures given on all subjects that
will increase our intelligence There are
different people who have made a life study
of special subjects, and if you sisters do not
feel like doing these things yourselves, call
upon those who are prepared to do this.
See if you can’t bring your attendance up
to the enrollment, urge all to join and
bring them up to the standard of excellence
that our Relief Society should come up to
and be progressive, se ure lecturers, etc.,
look after new conver s, they come here
and feel that they are alone, when in their
little branches the Elders look after them.
Try and make them feel as if they have
friends and draw them near to you, and
invite them to come out and join the Relief
Society and try aud make them feel con-
tented, and may we grow and increase in
knowledge and intelligence.
	President Jane S. Richards, of Weber
Stake, said we did not look like strangers
to her, she had been pleased with what had
been said. We have had good times in the
past and we will in the future. Spoke of
her experience as counselor to Sister Zina
and that she had wanted her to advise and
to talk more. She said, “Now, you who
are counselors stand right up to your
president and if she don’t see everything
advise her if you see things that she don’t
see. The first thing that was given me to
to do was to take care of the sick. Sisters,
take care of those who are sick whether in
your own house or your neighbor’s. Every
member or teacher will feel better when
they go to their meetings; and take the
children to meeting and bring the young
men to Relief Society meetings and let
them see and hear that it may be useful
when they go out into the world and are
called upon to assist or give counsel to the
sisters.” Related some incidents in her


[Column 1]
experience with Sister Eliza R. Snow when
visiting through Weber county.
	Mrs. Emma A. Empey, superintendent
of the Nurses’ class, presented a brief and
concise report in writing, which was very
well received and very appreciative of the
work which had been accomplished.
	Dr. Margaret C. Roberts made some pre-
liminary remarks and then read an ably
written paper entitled, “What shall we do
with our girls ? What shall we do with
our women ?”
Brother George D Pyper sang a solo,
“Sometime, Somewhere,” and was per-
suaded to sing again, “There is sunshine
in my soul today."
A vote of thanks was tendered to Prof.
C. J. Thomas, to Mrs. Hal J. Moore and
George D. Pyper for the excellent music
they had given, which had added so much
to our conference.
	Sister C. S. Williams explained the ten
cent fund and First Counselor Annie T.
Hyde gave notice of the pamphlet in pro-
cess of publication. Sister Emily S. Rich-
ards announced a suffrage meeting to be
held in the Assembly Hall at 11 o’clock a.
m. on Tuesday, April 8.
	Meeting was adjourned to half past
seven in the evening. Singing, Doxology.
Benediction by Sister Sarah Jenne Cannon.
	Evening sessson, half past seven, First
Counselor Annie T. Hyde presiding.
Singing, “Let us pray, gladly pray.”
Prayer by Sister Julia P. M. Farnsworth.
Singing, “I’ll praise my Maker while I’ve
breath.”
	Opening remarks were made by the pre-
siding officer. Counselor Annie T. Hyde, in
which she expressed regret at the absence
of the President and introduced the princi-
pal speaker of the evening, Dr. J. M. Tan-
ner, who made an eloquent address on
motherhood and the spirit of liberty and
of heavenly charity; giving many incidents
illustrative of the power and good effects of
generosity as opposed to the narrow and
selfish attributes of character; he com-
mended the work of the Relief Society, its
benevolent and uplifting character, and ex-
pressed his reverence for those who had
labored so long and faithfully in planting
good seed in the hearts of the young peo-
ple that had developed and brought forth
such good results; knew that such labors
were beneficial to the community and
broadened the people among whom they
ministered, diffusing common sympathy,
and uniting forces active for good.
Thought it dangerous to allow selfishness
to intrude into the home, generous feelings
were what would tend to a higher life, this
is a matter of psychology and should be
studied carefully in the child by the teach-
er. The Relief Society should encourage
the mother who in her home sets the ex-
ample of liberality and who will impart it
to her unborn children. Gave some illus-
trations and referred especially to the
generous qualities possessed by President
Joseph F. Smith, which endeared him to
his family and to the people, his tender-
ness in the home especially. Prof. Tan-
ner’s address was a glowing tribute to
those engaged in Relief Society work.
	Sister Margaret Hull rendered, in her
sweet voice, a solo, “Is the way so dark ?”
	Counselor Ida Smoot Dusenberry made
a brief address, explained in regard to the
Parents Class in Provo and spoke of the
way in which the Relief Society was in-

[Column 2]
troducing a new departure, and suggested
methods of improvement.
	Sister Alice M. Horne occupied a few
minutes in speaking upon art in the home,
made some excellent suggestions in regard
to harmony in furnishings and also of
color, said the people should be the princi-
pal part of the house, preferred rugs that
could be taken up and cleaned to car-
pets and advanced some good ideas in re-
gard to home-making.
	Counselor Annie T. Hyde presented the
matter of Nurses Classes to the presidents
of Stakes and made some practical sugges-
tions about the arrangements, Dr. Roberts
had promised to teach another class this,
year, said every girl who expects to be
a mother should take a course of these
lectures, whether she ever needs to be a
nurse or not, asked that all who wished
information on the subject would write to
her. Mrs. Dusenberry made the same re-
quest in regard to Parents Classes.
	Congregation sang the Doxology and
Conference was adjourned for six months,
to October, by the presiding officer, Mrs.
Hyde. Dismissed with prayer by Mrs. E.
B. Wells.
	E. B. WELLS, Secretary.


6 WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.												7


IN MEMORIAM.

ISABELLA WILLIAMS.

	It has become our painful duty to announce the
death of one of our beloved sisters of the Syracuse
Relief Society, Isabella Williams, wife of E. H.
Williams, aged thirty-nine years, who passed
away Sunday morning, April 27. She had been
troubled for several years with leakage of the
heart, but was confined to her bed only a few
days.
	The deceased was a faithful member of the
Relief Society since its first organization and filled
the office of visiting teacher. She was a model
wife and mother and endeared herself to those
around her by her kindness in times of sickness.
She possessed an affable, pleasant nature and won
the esteem and good feeling of her many acquain-
tances. She leaves a husband and seven sorrow-
ing children, who will feel the loss of an affection-
ate wife and loving mother until that joyful time
when they will again meet and enjoy her society
in a state far more happy and glorified.
	MARIA COOK, Sec.

RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT TO ELIZABETH T. SHELLEY.

	Whereas it has pleased our Heavenly Father
to call from us on May 24, 1902, our associate and
colaborer, Sister Elizabeth T. Shelley, who, al-
though having laid helpless on her bed for three
months with that dreadful disease, cancer, was
patient and cheerful to the last. She was first
vice-president in the 4th ward Relief Society of
American Fork, a sincere and earnest worker in
the cause of truth, a virtuous and loving wife,
a kind and faithful mother. She was the mother
of twelve children, six of whom survive her.
	Resolved, That we, the officers and members of
the society, feel the loss of such a faithful worker
in the cause of truth and that we humbly acknowl-
edge an over-ruling Power who doeth all things
well.
	That a copy of these resolutions be sent to the
husband and family and one be sent to the
“EXPONENT” for publication. 
ALICE G. STEELE,
NANCY B. FORBES,
SARAH H. SHELLEY,
	Committee
Relief Society, 4th ward American Fork.

HANNAH E. CROMPTON.

	A token of respect and remembrance of our be-
loved sister, Hannah E. Crompton, who departed
this life March 14, 1901. Sister Crompton was
born in Olden, Lancashire, England, 1815. She
came to this country in 1844, and was married
to Brother John Crompton in Council Bluffs, 
March 1, 1850. She held the position of second
counselor in the Relief Society in American Fork
for eighteen years. As a teacher, wife, mother
and friend her example was worthy of imitation.
She was one of the noblest of God’s handmaidens


and was faithful through all trials and tribulations 
that beset her pathway. And we, as members of 
the Relief Society of the Freedom Ward of San-
[-]ete Stake of Zion, form the following resolutions: 
	RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT. 
	Whereas God has seen fit to call from this life 
our beloved sister, Hannah E. Crompton,
	Resolved, That we mourn in her death the loss 
of a faithful worker, a comforter in the hour of 
trouble, a devoted wife and mother and a true 
Latter-day Saint. 
That we esteem her faithful labors worthy of 
emulation by every member of the society, that 
we sympathize and mourn with the aged husband, 
the children, grandchildren, great grandchildren 
and friends. 
That we send a copy of these resolutions to the 
WOMAN'S EXPONENT for publication, that a copy 
be kept in the society and that one be given to her 
husband, Brother John Crompton. 
MARY E. SIMONSEN, 
FLORENCE LOWRY, 
NELLIE DRAPER.


ANNIVERSARY CELEBRATION. 

	We wish to inform you that our little
village of Freedom held its anniversary 
meeting on Monday, March 18, 1902, to 
commemorate the organization of the 
Relief Society at Nauvoo in 1842. 
	On account of the loss of our beloved
sister, Hannah Crompton, our gathering
was partly to pay our tribute of respect to
her as well as the anniversary.
	The meeting was called to order by
President Mary E. Simonsen. Singing,
"Come ye children of the Lord." Prayer
by Brother Joseph Draper. Singing,
"Weary not." Roll was called, minutes
read and approved Welcome speech by
President Mary E. Simonsen, who said we
had met in commemoration of the organiza-
tion of the Relief Society by the Prophet
Joseph Smith, and read a few lines from
his teachings. She spoke in high esteem
of our departed sister, Hannah E. Cromp-
ton.
	Song by Sister Mattie Draper and com-
pany. Select reading from the WOMAN'S
EXPONENT by Sister Florence Lowry.
Reading by Sister Phoebe Draper. Reading
of resolutions of respect in memory of
Sister Hannah E. Crompton, by Sister
Emily Taylor.
	Brother John Crompton spoke to the
sisters, encouraging them to continue in 
the good work; also read a selection en-
titled "The Grove." Recitation by Sister
Minnie Lowry. Song by Sister Jane
Hunt.
	Bishop William Lowry spoke very en-
couragingly in behalf of the Relief Society,
also in kind remembrance of Sister Cromp-
ton.
	Brother Almon Draper also felt well in
being privileged to meet with the society.
Remarks by Brother Joseph Draper. 
	Meeting adjourned for one year. Sing-
ing. Benediction by Brother Almon
Draper.
		EMILY TAYLOR, Sec. pro tem.


A DELIGHTFUL GATHERING. 
	Our Relief Society of Parowan have
taken up a very commendable labor, that
of taking a meeting to the aged or en-
feebled, not able to attend the regular
sessions They arrange a program and
take refreshments, and if necessary do
sewing for the parties they visit. Very
happy results are realized, and in this way
it becomes established that our organiza-
tion is a power for good.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT


Yesterday, May 20, a public social gath-
ering convened in honor of one of our aged 
sisters, Phebe Benson, who was the first
president of the Relief Society of this ward, 
and has ever been an active, energetic 
worker in all righteous causes, in fact, she 
has been a dear mother to the Parowan
community.
	The program consisted of a biographical 
sketch of Sister Benson, speeches, songs,
recitations, etc.; the most beautiful feature 
being the song, “Be Kind to the Old
Folks,” by her children, grandchildren and 
great grandchildren. An Album was pre-
sented in behalf of the Relief Society, in 
which the biographical sketch is to be 
written and the autograph of every mem-
ber of the Relief Society. 
Sister Benson responded very feelingly, 
extending her love and blessing to every 
creature in the ward. She said she felt 
that she was connected to all and her heart 
was filled with blessings for all. 
Grandma Benson was tastily dressed, and 
in her beautifully decorated seat of honor, 
looked as grand as a queen on her throne, 
and indeed she was queen of hearts, such a 
unity of spirit is rarely enjoyed. 
Sister Benson is on the verge of her 
eighty-second birthday, is the mother of 
eight children, has forty-five grandchildren
and sixteen great grandchildren. Her 
faculties are bright and she is still indus-
trious. At the present time she is em-
broidering Temple aprons and doing other 
light work; and if one is needing counsel, 
it is safe to go to Grandma Benson, who 
has truly “grown old beautifully.”
	E. CRANE WATSON.


"THE GIPSY QUEEN." 
	The operetta, "The Gipsy Queen," pre-
sented in the Opera House at Provo by the 
Brigham Young Academy kindergarten 
department, was one of the most beautiful 
entertainments imaginable, and shows 
wonderful talent and willingness to learn 
on the part of the little folks, and at the 
same time reflecting great credit on their 
teachers. The leading roles--"Rosalie," 
"The Fairy Queen" and "Mother Grunt," 
assumed by Miss Margaret Dusenberry, 
Miss Lorie Eggertsen and Miss Jessie 
Ramsey, respectively, were well sustained. 
Little Miss Dusenberry had a very lengthy 
part and won the hearts of the audience by 
her good singing and earnest work. In 
Miss Ramsey's second role, that of a boy, 
her fine contralto voice was heard to good 
advantage, and the duet by her and little 
Miss Margaret was very prettily sung and 
cleverly acted. 
Miss Agnes Clive and Miss Ethel Beasley, 
little girls from Salt Lake City, apparently 
about seven or eight years of age, took the 
house by storm with their specialties, Miss 
Clive as a violinist and Miss Beasley as a
dancer.
	The choruses were fine, and the scenic 
effects, costumes, etc., were very beautiful.

RED CROSS CONFERENCE. 
	St. Petersburg, June 5.-- The Inter-
national Red Cross conference has ad-
journed. The conference passed resolu-
tions urging all countries to prohibit the 
use by manufacturers for commercial pur-
poses of the Red Cross symbols and to give 
manufacturers six months' notice of this
decision.
	After relating instances in which the 
Red Cross privileges have been abused, 
the Belgian International Ambulance corps
resolved that every state is bound to exer-
cise strict supervision over the private relief 
expeditions organized in its territory; that 
no such organization should leave the state 
without authorization from the government, 
and that such expeditions should not be 
allowed to use the Red Cross unless they 
have been recognized by some Red Cross 
society. The Belgian International Ambu-
lance Corps recommended the establish-
ment of international bureaus to supply in-
formation concerning prisoners of war, aid 
societies, etc.; that the maritime Red Cross
stations should agree to receive all the 
wounded and sick persons gathered by 
hospital ships without distinction of nation-
ality; that it was desirable for the Red 
Cross societies to prepare for relief in time
of war by active service in time of peace 
and finally, that the powers take steps to 
bring about the execution of article 10 of 
The Hague convention, which has not yet 
been ratified. 
At the last dinner given in honor of the 
delegates to the conference, Prof. Martens, 
professor of international law at the 
University of St. Petersburg, referred in 
glowing terms to the humane arrangements 
that exist in American cities for the care of 
the public. Mrs. W. P. Foster rreplied to 
Prof Martens in a graceful address.


NOTES AND NEWS. 
	We are too fond of our own will. We 
want to be doing what we fancy mighty
things; but the great point is to do small
things, when called to them, in a right 
spirit.- R. CECIL. 
	The Women's National Liberal Federation 
of England has voted to support no candi-
dates for Parliament except those who will 
pledge themselves, if elected, to vote for 
full suffrage for women. 
	Miss Jane Addams was severely injured 
last week in a wreck on the Santa Fe 
railroad at Revere, Mo., on her way home 
from Los Angeles. She was seated with 
her nephew in the last car. The train
while runing at high speed was derailed 
by striking a switch. Miss Addams was 
nearly buried in the wreckage, and when 
she was rescued it was found that her face 
was badly lacerated and her left arm in-
jured. 
	Florence Nightingale celebrated her eigh-


ty first birthday, May 15, at Claydon House 
Buckinghamshire, the country seat of her 
sister, Lady Verney. There was no pub-
lic demonstration, just an informal gather-
ing of a few of her friends. A striking 
instance of the love and veneration felt for 
Miss Nightingale by English soldiers was 
lately given by a contemporary: The late 
Sir John Steell, sculptor to Queen Victoria, 
had modelled a bust of Miss Nightingale. 
An officer of one of the Highland regiments 
which had suffered so cruelly in the Cri-
mean heard that the bust had just been 
completed and was in Steell's studio. 
Many of the men in his company had 
passed through the hospital at Scutari, and 
he got permission from the sculptor to 
bring some of them to see the bust. Ac-
cordingly, a squad marched into the studio 
and stood in line. They had no idea why 
they had been mustered in so strange a 
place. The bust was uncovered and then, 
as by one impulse, the men broke rank 
and with cries of "Miss Nightingale! Miss 
Nightingale!" surrounded the model, and 
with hats off, cheered the figure of their 
devoted nurse until the roof rang. Like 
Clara Barton, Florence Nightingale has 
been a lifelong advocate of equal rights for 
women. Years ago she wrote "You ask 
my reasons for believing in woman's suf-
frage. It seems to me almost self-evident, 
an axiom, that every tax-payer ought to 
have a voice in the expenditure of the 
money pay, involving, as this does, inter-
ests that are the most vital to a human
being.”

LET THERE BE PEACE 
You question, what can woman do 
To spread the day of peace? 
Can she demand disarmament, 
Or bid a world cease 
That which for ages it maintained, 
A dire necessity, 
To secure the rights of nations 
And obtain equity ?

Oh, what shall woman do indeed, 
Shall she stand helpless by 
With tight-clasped hands and aching heart 
While her offspring bleed and die ?
Shall she waste time in tears and sighs 
For the "golden age" of man, 
And not so much as lift her hand 
To consummate the plan. 
Which God decreed in His high heaven, 
And prophets long foretold; 
Should crown the earth in latter days 
And all mankind enfold?

O, what cannot a woman do, 
When once she knows the right!
With heart and hand and lifted voice, 
She'll demonstrate her might 
In love and courage, wisdom, too. 
As time her powers increase, 
For this is truly woman's age 
And woman's age brings peace. 
What can a woman do, you ask,
With a baby at her knee? 
O, blessed boon, for therein lies 
Thine opportunity; 
Seize it, and teach thy children all 
To live the Golden Rule, 
That Jesus lives, the Prince of Peace. 
That earth is His footstool, 
Whereon He will not rest His feet, 
While marred with strife and blood; 
That all must work, both great and small 
For universal good. 
Then He will come, O glor'ous day, 
And war will be no more; 
But peace and love and brotherhood 
Abound from shore to shore. 
Fling out your banners to the breeze, 
Yellow, purple and white;
Let all mankind take up the theme, 
God said, "Let there be light," 
And so say we. Let there be peace. 
For light and peace are one; 
Then earth will be a paradise, 
Her glory like the sun. 
RUTH M. FOX. 
May, 1902.

from the East where she has been visiting
relatives and friends in Washington, Balti-
more, Chicago, Minneapolis and Decorah. 
We will publish some account of her visit in 
our August number.


MAKE MY HEART BRAVE !

	Make my heart brave, O Lord !
My arm is weak, but strong enough withal 
To do my duty whatsoe'er thy call; 
But, oh, my arm doth hesitate and fall, 
Knowing the the weakness of my fainting heart. 
	Make my heart brave, O Lord !
I had so dreamed of doing nobler deeds; 
But I must minister to lesser needs; 
Only Thou knowest how ambition pleads! 
Make my heart brave to do the little things.

	Make my heart brave, O Lord !
Is this the path that I have chosen long ?
How fair that other road! The thought breeds
	wrong,
Help me to lift my burden with a song; 
Make my heart brave to walk the unchosen
		ways.

Make my heart brave, O Lord !
Brave for the loss of faces dearer grown; 
Brave for the battle that is mine alone; 
The glory unachieved I thought mine own, 
Make not my burden less; make my heart
		brave!
ANNIE PIKE.

	Mrs. Elizabeth Stuart Phelps Ward is 
credited with the authorship of "The Con-
fessions of a Wife," the anonymous story 
now appearing in the Century Magazine. 
	Mrs. William Tod Helmuth, president 
of the National Council of Women, has 
gone abroad for the summer and possibly 
part of the winter. She expects to be 
present at the meeting of the International 
Council at Copenhagen. 
	Lady Henry Somerset expects to sail for 
New York on September 26. Unable to 
remain long in this country she has 
pledged herself to few engagements be-
yond the National Convention of the 
Woman's Christian Temperance Union at 
Portland, Me. She will visit Montreal, 
Washington and Boston, in each of which 
cities she will deliver an address. She 
will be accompanied by Miss Cameron,


Anna Grant  Rhoda Miller  Annie Hansen  Sybella Clayton  Ellen Peterson  Helen Whitney

	The students in this picture are representative, in a certain sense, of the girls in 
other Church schools throughout the several Stakes of Zion, and are devoted to the
highest educational work, that will fit and prepare them to take the places and positions 
in public life as educators, etc., or in private life as mothers, teachers of the young, 
housewives, and in fact fill all the requirements of full and complete womanhood. Much 
more is expected of the daughters of Zion than of the women outside in the world; and 
consequently the teachers as well as the parents have greater responsibility resting upon 
them. This matter is taken into consideration when selecting the teachers in these edu-
cational institutions. The young girls in these Church schools, colleges and universities, 
are, generally speaking, of that highly moral and religious type that is only found in com-
munities where righteousness prevails and the best principles are carefully taught and mu-
tual helpfulness maintained, and where the saying of our Savior is most carefully observed, 
viz., "To do unto others as we would have them do unto us."

MRS. HORNE HOME. 
HAS RETURNED FROM CHICAGO AFTER A 
MONTH'S ABSENCE. 
Hon. Alice Merrill Horne has just re-
turned from Chicago where she has been 
staying for a month past, the guest of Miss 
Josephine C. Locke, former supervisor of 
drawing in the Chicago public schools.
Mrs. Horne visited the public schools there 
and brought home a variety of drawing for 
comparison with our local school work. 
She met a number of the leading teach-

[Column 2]
ers, educators and artists of that city and 
attended noted lectures at the University of
Chicago, and the Chicago institute of Art. 
Instructions were sent by the present 
officers of the Relief Society to Mrs. Horne 
to meet with the Relief Society members of 
the Chicago branch. President Asahel 
Woodruff accordingly called a meeting 
which convened on July 9, at the L. D. S. 
house of worship on Oakley Avenue. 
There was a good attendance. The fol-
lowing officers were set apart by President 
Woodruff: Caroline Dennison, president; 
Signature


Sarah Cress, Charlotte Estherbloom, coun-
selors; Josephine Turner, secretary; Mary
Post, treasurer. It was decided to hold a
meeting once a month on the regular fast
day, when a proper subject will be studied.
Then one meeting a week for sewing and
planning relief work is to be held at the
homes of the workers of the branch. Next
Wednesday the sisters will meet at the
home of the president.
	Mrs. Horne addressed the sisters on the
subject of the duties of the Relief Society
members and gave a short sketch of the
life and work of President Bathsheba W.
Smith, as it is and has been connected with
Relief Society work; also a sketch of Mrs.
E. B. Wells, who has been General
Secretary for so many years. Mrs. Horne
told of the work being done by Mrs. Hyde,
Dusenberry and Williams, with the idea
of bringing the Relief members of the
Chicago branch more closely in touch with
the General officers here. The sisters of
the branch expressed themselves as being
interested in the relief work and are de-
sirous of knowing something of the plans
and assist in erecting the proposed woman's
building.
