[Column 1]
THREE QUARTERS OF A CENTURY.
Dedication of Relief Society Stake House
WEBER STAKE OF ZION. : 
JULY 19, 1902.
(Conclusion.)
	Afternoon session, President Jane S.
Richards presiding. On the stand were
many veteran brethren and sisters
	Meeting opened by singing, “Now let
us rejoice in the day of Salvation.” Prayer
by President L. W. Shurtliff. Singing,
“Glorious things are sung of Zion.”
	The speakers of the afternoon were
President Jane S. Richards, who gave a
little sketch of the thought and purpose of
the building, and alluded feelingly to those
who had assisted with means and encourage-
ment; mentioned in this connection the
liberality of Brother D. H. Perry, (since
departed) his generous gift, and that of his
wife afterwards, and Bishop Bernard
White, who had given the land, spoke
beautifully of Mrs. Fred Kiesel, who,
though not a “Mormon” had noticed Mrs.
Richards ministering to all in need of all
faiths, or no faith, and thanked all who
had in any way contributed towards
the erection of the hall and its furnishing.
	During the afternoon a check was re-
ceived by Mrs. Richards from a friend, of one
hundred dollars, and a modest tribute was
paid to Brothers F. S. and C. C. Richards,
who had given so liberally. Sister Richards
stated that a telegram of congratulations 
had been received from Franklin S. Rich-

[Column 2]
ards and his wife, who are on a visit in
California.
	Too much cannot be said in praise of the
labors of this energetic, devout and far-
seeing President of the Relief Society of the
Weber Stake of Zion. Sister Richards de-
serves the fullest appreciation of the people
among whom she has labored so industri-
ously and diligently. Assuredly she has
fulfilled Solomon’s words, “She stretcheth
out her hand to the poor, she reacheth
forth her hand to the needy;” and all must
admit “her own works praise her within
the gates.”
	Sister Richards referred lovingly to the
sisters who had cooporated with her in her
labors in the Stake and gave due credit to
the efforts made by them in helping on
the building. The hall stands a landmark,
a monument for the future of the self-
sacrifice and noble courage of the sisters in
attempting such an undertaking without
money in the treasury.
	President M. I. Horne, of Salt Lake
Stake, and Sarah J. Cannon, her first
counselor, Mary E. Lightner, of Miners-
ville, one of the oldest members in the
Church and a powerful speaker, Annie T.
Hyde, First Counselor in the General
Relief Society, Emmeline B. Wells General
Secretary in the Relief Soeiety, and Presi-
dent Bathsheba W. Smith, of the General
Relief Society were the other sisters who
made brief remarks upon the great work of
the society organized by the Prophet Joseph
so long ago, and its rapid advancement
since that period, commending the labors
of Sister Richards both as a presiding
officer for twenty-five years and her ex-
ecutive ability in managing affairs, and in
the building of the first Relief Society
Stake House
	President John R. Winder, Patriarch
John Smith, Bishop McKay of Huntsville,
Bishop Bernard White and some other
elderly brethren made brief remarks, there
was some fine music by young lady sing-
ers and altogether the exercises of the day
were harmoniously in keeping with the
spirit of the Latter-day work. At the
close the Doxology was sung by the con-
gregation, and the benediction was pro-
nounced by President John R. Winder.


A BOOK FOR THE HOLIDAYS.
Eleven years ago and more, the writer
obtained the copyright of a book of stories
she had written for publication. Circum-
stances prevented bringing out the book at
the time, and although repeated efforts
to publish have been made since none have
succeeded. The writer has now decided to
publish by subscription if a sufficient num-
ber of persons can be secured who are will-
ing to pay beforehand, so as to give the
publishers a guarantee to undertake the
work. The book will be entitled “Aunt
Em’s Stories of Long Ago.” It will con-
tain “Hephzibah,” which caused quite a
sensation when it appeared as a serial in
1889’ “Aunt Esther’s Sweetheart,” “Some
Old Love Letters,” “He Cometh Not,”
and other short stories. The stories are all
founded upon facts—of course the names
are fictitious, necessarily.
	Any one desiring further particulars may
obtain them by writing to this office. If
possible to raise the money the book will
be on sale at Christmas and during the
holidays. Any money taken in will be
receipted for and returned if the book is not
issued.


DAUGHTERS OF THE REVOLUTION.

	The Utah Society of the Daughters of
the Revolution were entertained in Septem-
ber (the 17th) at the home of Mrs. Lewis
M. Cannon. The rooms were prettily dec-
orated for the occasion with the Colonial
colors in which the gorgeous sunflowers
were most conspicuous, the blue vases and
bowls taking the second piece in the dis-
play.
	The meeting was a very interesting one,
the first after the adjournment during the hot
weather, and, in fact, the first with the new
officers. The paper was by Mrs. Isabel M.
W. Sears and treated of that period of the
Revolution, events of 1778-1779. “The
alliance of France. Proposals of peace
made by England. Battle of Monmouth.
Attack of Newport. French fleet sails to
West Indies. English occupy Savannah.
Massacre of Wyoming and Cherry Valleys.
British capture Augusta. Siege of Savan-
nah. Recapture of Stony Point. Paul
Jones and U. S. Navy.”
	The paper was read by Mrs. Lucy W.
Smith, as Mrs. Sears could not be present.
The Paul Jones and United States navy
portions were freely discussed and com-
mented upon, and extracts will be published
in this paper later on.
	After the meeting adjourned the conver-
sation became general and chocolate, cream
and delicious dainties were served and a
delightful time enjoyed by the members
and guests present.

	October 16 the Utah Society was enter-
tained by Mrs. Myra Y. Rossiter and a
delightful afternoon was spent. The ele-
gant parlors were festooned with flags and
everywhere the stars and strips and colonial
colors were in evidence. Autumn flowers
in abundance were used in decorating.
	The paper was by Mrs. Caroline Y. Can-
non and was read by her daughter, Mrs.
Willard T. Cannon.
	The paper contained, events of 1780-
1781. “Siege of Charleston. Battle of
Camden. Exploits of Marion, Sumpter,
Lee and Pickens. Battle of King’s Moun-
tain. Treason of Arnold. Battle of Cow-
pens. Green’s retreat through the Caro-
linas. Battle of Guilford Court House.
Siege of Yorktown. Close of the war.”
	After the reading of the paper some com-
ments were made, all complimentary to the
writer and reader. Mrs. Julia F. Lund
related what she had heard in Philadelphia
concerning Mrs. Arnold’s ignorance of her
husband’s treachery. It is said of her that
she would have remained in the United
States but for the intense bitterness which
it was thought might result disastrously; on
that account she was induced to leave her
home and friends.
Delicious refreshments, ices, etc., were
served, and the usual enlivening conversa-
tion indulged in, and the guests and mem-
bers of the society enjoyed a pleasant hour
of intellectual recreation.


A WORK OF ART.
	By courtesy of Miss Ada Patterson we
have received the new book recently issued,
entitled “By the Stage Door.” The book
is very handsomely bound and the entire
work is done in the latest style, typograph-
ically, and taken as a whole, is artistic.
	Miss Ada Patterson and Miss Victory
Bateman collaborated in preparing it for the
press, and the book bears the two names,


‘‘Patterson-Bateman.” The Introduction
is by Annie A. Adams, well and popularly
known in Utah, though now a resident of 
New York City. In closing she says: “I
recommend this book to every seeker after
knowledge of the stage, and to every
student of character, be he such a seeker or
not.”
	The opening is with a singularly beauti-
ful poem called “Life,” by Victory Bate-
man.
	The contents are ‘‘The Story of a Friend-
ship,” ‘‘Autobiography of an Actress,”
‘‘The Passing of the ‘Leading Lady,’ ”
‘‘Her One Superstition,” Grimston’s
School of Acting,” ‘‘A Triumph of Tem-
perament,” ‘‘He Married an Actress,”
‘‘The Understudy,” “Heard at the Manag-
er’s Club,” “An Early Jump,” “Three
Friends.”
	After a cursory perusal of the book one
feels how much temptation lies in the way
of those who make the stage a profession.
There are many passages of striking merit
in the sketches, and truths portrayed that
touch the heart with strong emotions of
tenderness and sweet sympathy. The
mother who reads the pages would almost
invariably say, “The stage is not for my
daughter or my child,”—son or daughter.
	There is a great deal of character study
in the work, it is a book to make one think
deeply. It might easily be called a psychol-
ogical srudy, and we cannot help repeating
here, it is indeed a work of art. There is
great depth of meaning, there is uncommon
talent and such tact displayed in making a
harmonious whole as one seldom finds in a
book of short stories. The language is
delicate and refined and one need not be
afraid to trust the reading of it to an inno-
cent, inexperienced girl; in fact the book is
good enough, in language and sentiment,
and sufficiently beautiful in outward appear-
ance to be placed on the drawing robm table
in any home, be it cottage or palatial man-
sion.
	These young ladies who have made a
first venture into the world of books are to be
congratulated on the merits of their literary
work, and let us hope the financial results
will be as satisfactory as the literary and
artistic work is genuine.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT


[Column 1]
	RELIEF SOCIETY CONFERENCE

	The General Relief Society Conference
was held in the Salt Lake Assembly Hall,
in this city, commencing at 2 p. m. on
Tuesday, October 7, 1902, President Bath-
sheba W. Smith presiding.
	Counselors Annie T. Hyde and Ida
Smoot Dusenberry, Secretary Emmeline B.
Wells and Treasurer Clarissa S. Williams
were on the stand,also President Jane S. Rich-
ards of Weber Stake, President M. I. Horne
of Salt Lake Stake, and her counselor, Sarah
Jenne Cannon, President Susan Grant of
Davis Stake, and many other presidents
and representatives of Stakes and a num-
ber of prominent sisters occupied seats on
the platform.
	Meeting opened by members of the
Temple choir singing “Our God we raise to
Thee,” Prof. C. J. Thomas leader. Prayer
was offered by Sister Susan Grant. Sing-
ing, trio, “My God the spring of all my
joys.” All the General Officers were pres-
ent and thirteen members of the General
Board, forty-three Stakes responded to roll
call. Brief minutes of the morning session

[Column 2]
of the April Conference were read and
approved.
	President Bathsheba W. Smith gave the
opening address, said she was delighted to
see so many assembled in conference; felt
she would like to know all the presidents
in the Stakes and in the wards, but could
not visit much on account of time,
because her calling was in the Temple; she
had been associated in that work ever since
she was a young girl, as it were, in this
and other Temples. ‘‘I want to bear testi-
mony to you that Joseph Smith introduced
the endowments, he taught us these things
and I received the ordinances during his
lifetime in Nauvoo. I attended every
meeting afterwards as long as he lived; the
same ordinances were given in the Nauvoo
Temple after the death of Joseph the
Prophet. I had the privilege of administer-
ing there, and for many years in the En-
dowment House in this city and in other
Temples here, and in this Temple now
nearly ten years, and that seems to be my
calling, and therefore I cannot visit very
much among the sisters, as this is more my
work than anything else. We have had a
grand conference and many great and good
teachings, and nothing has struck me more
forcibly than the converting of the Indians.
I have great sympathy for them; I let my
only son go to preach life and salvation to
them and he lost his life; and I believe he
is preaching to them yet in the spirit world;
I often think about Moroni, he brought the
Book of Mormon, and if we could see him,
he would like to have us do a work for his
people. I feel this and I love to hear of the
labor among that people.” President Smith
told the sisters that forty-two Stakes had
been visited since last conference; she
wished the sisters to be united, to take
care of the sick and the needy and to attend
to all the duties of the society; and she
wished them to subscribe for the EXPO-
NENT and to help it along and to read it,
she had always done so; and she asked “the
blessing of the Lord upon the Relief Soci-
ety and all engaged in the good work of
redemption. Amen.”
	The treasurer then read the report of
money on hand for the Building and also
all money donated from the several Stakes
for membership, and expenditures for
traveling expenses and other pur-
poses for the use of the society. The
treasurer’s report was received and ap-
proved.
	The Stakes were called and reported as
follows:
	Alberta, by First Counselor Rhoda Hin-
man, who made a good report and men-
tioned the fact that the Stake had recently
been divided; she rejoiced in having this
opportunity to speak for the people of
Canada.
	Miss Amelia Carling, missionary from
the Northwestern States mission, spoke for
the branch of the Relief Society in Kansas
City, which had nineteen members.
The past year the sisters had by work
made $122,25, had spent part of it; she
felt proud to be connected with the society.
About a year ago Sisters S. J. Cannon and
A. T. Hyde had paid a visit to the Relief
Society branches in that mission and had
done a great deal of good.
	Sister Minnie K. Robinson reported the
Relief Society branches in the California
mission: in Sacramento there was a fine
society, in San Francisco they were wide
awake; at first they had an idea they

[Column 3]
must not do anything but work; thought
the sisters appreciated the meetings of the
society more than we do in Zion, in Los
Angelos the society is in working order
now, and in San Bernardino the meetings
have been discontinued, but may be re-
sumed again when there are more members
in the Church. Her report was very satis-
factory.
	Chicago was reported by Sister Charlotte
Estherbloom, first counselor in the Society
before coming to Salt Lake, said among
the two or three hundred members in the
Church there were many poor people that
had to be helped, and the society also
helped the Elders, she felt that they had
been blest. Every fall they had a bazar
and took in two or three hundred dollars,
all the sisters were interested in the work,
they were united in peace and harmony.
	Sister Lucy B. Young represented the
branches of the society in Germany, one in
Hamburg, one in Stettin and one in Berlin
She had been appointed to preside over
them but could not speak much to them in
their native tongue. Brother Loyd Wood-
ruff interpreted for her, and other Elders.
She urged the mothers to have their boys
know something concerning Relief Society
work, so that when upon missions they
would be able to render assistance to these
organizations out in the world. Com-
mended Sister Jane S. Richards for having
taken her boys to the sisters’ meetings.
Sister Young spoke highly of the German
sisters and their loyalty to the work of the
Lord and said how anxious they were to
learn, and become intelligent.
	Counselor Annie T. Hyde reported
Union Stake, as there was no representative
present and she had recently visited the
conference in La Grande, Oregon, June 14.
Said it was a most beautiful country, they
did not have to irrigate the land, it was
wonderfully easy to raise their grain, etc.
The Saints there were trying to set a good
example, and they had a fine society, a
strong president, who had been in the
Relief Society work previously in Nephi,
and she seemed very capable and energetic;
thought they were likely to make rapid
improvement.
	Counselor Ida Smoot Dusenberry re-
ported the branch of the society in New
York, there the younger girls seemed to
take quite an active part in the society,
after the testimony meeting, they have a
program and invite others to come in; they
have a number of strangers in their meet-
ing who seem interested and there is a
united feeling, hoped the Lord would bless
them in this work.
	Fremont Stake reported by First Counse-
lor Elizabeth Atkinson, who said: “I live
where we have babies in our meetings and
when we have them I believe we have
angels.” She gave an excellent report,
particularly concerning the grain.
	Juab Stake was reported by Counselor
Letitia Teasdale, said it had only been two
months organized, but the officers had
visited every branch, except one, and found
them in good condition. “We are united
with our Bishops and the presiding brethren,
and young and old are trying to do all they
can towards the accomplishment of the
Lord’s work. We have spoken about the
saving of grain, and feel that a greater
interest is being awakened in this and other
important things.
	Sister Annie C. Layton responded to the
call for St. Joseph Stake, said the president


[Column 1]
had asked her to report, or at least to
answer to the roll call; she felt that in
that far off place the sisters labored under
many difficulties not realized by those who
were in localities where there were many
greater advantages; she felt great embar-
rassment in attempting to report the soci-
ety, felt sure the sisters in St Joseph Stake
were trying to do right, and they have an
energetic president.
	San Luis Stake reported by the secretary,
Sister Mary F. Crowther. “We live a long
way off; we would like to have the sisters visit
us; we have a good Relief Society hall, the
only public building there is there, three
wards out of five have their own house to
meet in. Our Young Ladies’ Association
put an organ in our hall, we all meet in it.
We have our grain in the tithing granary,
but we intend having a granary of our
own. The Lord has blessed us greatly in
that land. We had a visit from Sister E.
B. Wells and Sister Minnie J. Snow three
years ago, and we had a delightful time.”
	San Juan Stake reported by Sister Pris-
cilla Warner, of Moab. “Our Stake is very
remote, we have to travel four hundred
miles by team, it is very laborious; our
president, Sister Hammond, is a very faith-
ful woman. I think our sisters are all try-
ing to do all they possibly can; we labor
under many difficulties but we want to do
our duty and keep the commandments of
God.”
	Uintah Stake reported by Sister Sarah
M. Smith, Colton. “We are a long way off,
it seems we have very few advantages, and
we have to trust in the Lord and depend
upon His assistance; we are united and
things are moving on, and I think some
little progress is being made; we had a
visit this summer from the sisters, which
was a great blessing; two years ago we
had the sisters, and we do appreciate them
in this out of the way place.”
	President Julia A. Richards, who had
only recently been called to preside in
Malad Stake, said they were determined to
do their duty and to be in harmony with
the priesthood and keep up with organiza-
tions in the other Stakes of Zion, felt very
great need of encouragement in her labors,
etc.
	President M. Isabella Horne said she felt
greatly exercised because of the drouth all
over the country, and thought if we didn’t
have a famine among ourselves, we want
to and ought to have wheat on hand any
way to assist those who will be in need,
and not keep the money; she had spoken
over and over to the sisters to buy wheat
with their money instead of putting it in
the bank, for what good would money do
when scarcity came; spoke of Heber C.
Kimball’s prophecies in regard to these
things; she thought all the branches in
this Stake were in good order, even the
new ones organized since the Stake had
been divided; she held officers’ meeting
once a month and therefore knew the cir-
cumstances of each society pretty well;
some sisters do not identify themselves
with the society, but thought these times
would stir up the people to greater diligence.
	President Ann C. Woodbury, St. George
Stake, was glad of the sisters visiting them
and among the people; until last sum-
mer, a year ago, they had been a long time
without being visited from headquarters,
this last summer they had been visited
again and it had a good effect. She had
been making a long trip recently into the

[Column 2]
White River country and found in those
isolated places the people were trying to
live their religion They depended on the
land for their living. Many young people
had married and gone into that country to
make themselves homes and the Lord had
blessed them. Spoke of the good spirit
prevailing among those who were striving
to serve the Lord.
	Conference adjourned until 7 p. m.
Singing, “My God, the spring of all my
joys.” Benediction by Elder C. J. Thomas.

	Evening session, Tuesday, October 7,
1902, President Bathsheba W. Smith pre-
siding.
	Singing, “Our God we raise to Thee.”
Prayer by President Jane S. Richards.
Singing, “We thank Thee, O God, for a
prophet,”
	Benson Stake reported by the president,
Rebecca Rawlins. Reported they had
visited their Stake and found all right
among the sisters; in regard to mothers’
classes “we decided to make up a pro-
gram and unite with the three associations
and hold conjoint meetings.”
	Counselor Anna Bayliss, Parowan Stake.
“We have five branches, we hold semi-
monthly meetings and feel much good has
been accomplished. We have had much
sickness this year. Sister Ollerton, our
president, is an excellent woman and very
much beloved.”
	Counselor Alvira E. Steele, Bingham
Stake, reported 28 branches, spoke very
highly of the president, Sister E. J. Ben-
nett, also said there was a lot of good sis-
ters. Most of the branches own a city lot,
one branch owns its own house “We are
united and want to have our sisters visit us
and I hope we will have sufficient means,
will do our utmost to get the sisters to
attend the nurses’ class.”
	Star Valley reported by Sister Charlotte
Allred, who said it inspired her with new
life to see our dearly beloved sisters; “we
are trying to do our best, and I feel our
sisters are in pretty good condition,” etc.
	Counselor Lucy Parkinson represented
Oneida Stake. Said, “Our sisters are dili-
gent in the storing of grain, the secretaries’
reports in our Stake are excellent, we have
a very able president and we hope to help
in the work and be in harmony with those
who preside.”
	President Mary E. Howell spoke for
Hyrum Stake. “We have over 4,000
bushels of grain, we are united with the
president of our Stake. We had a visit
from Sister Hyde and Sister Freeze, and
were very glad of the good things they told
us, felt benefited thereby. We think the
mothers’ classes will be very beneficial.
We want to keep up with all the require-
ments made upon us. We want to send
some sisters to study nursing.”
	President Jane S. Richards was glad to
feel so much of the good spirit. Refering
to Weber Stake, she said, “We are trying to
instil faith into the minds of the young,
many sick have been saved by prayer.
We need to love each other and give kindly
aid, there are so many worldly things
around and we are only here for a little
while and we want to do all we can while
we have the opportunity. We have built a
Relief Society Stake house and we have not
neglected the sick or needy, but have kept
up our donations for these things and have
not gone into debt; the house cost about
$5,000, and it is not in any way extrava-

[Column 3]
gant, it is neat and plain. We have no
heat or light in it yet, but we expect to fix
for that before we need it The Y. L. and
P. A. meet in it as well as the Relief
Society, it is for all three. We have some
pictures on the walls—Joseph the Prophet,
Brigham Young, John Taylor, Sister Eliza,
Aunt Zina,. Sister Bathsheba and some
others. We had our house dedicated last
summer, the brethren came up and some of
the sisters. President J. R. Winder and
Patriarch John Smith were with us and we
had it dedicated on the anniversary of the
day that President Young was in Ogden
and established the sisters’ quarterly confer-
ences. There were other brethren and
sisters there, those who have gone before
us, let us live so that we can join them in
the spirit world. Let us reach out a help-
ing hand to those who are weak and way-
ward. It is terrible to know that the chil-
dren of the Saints should incline to go
astray ! If we can persuade them to turn
around what a satisfaction it would be!
Working for the dead is glorious, but they
have not had the Gospel and rejected it.
Let us be charitable and forgiving and
seek to save the erring.”
	Counselor Lavinia Bates reported Cassia
Stake, said they had not yet had mothers’
classes but were trying to do all they could
to keep up with the other organizations in
caring for the needy, saving grain and all
good works.
	President Elizabeth DeLong, Panguitch
Stake, said, “We will send all the
sisters we can to the nurses’ class. We
have visited our Stake once this summer
and have started a mothers’ class;” told of
other good works.
	President Lucy S. Cardon Cache Stake.
“Since the Stake has been divided it has
made some difference about the grain. I
know it is the grain we want and not the
money. We are advocating the nurses’
class and working it up and want to con-
vert the mothers.”
	Counselor Dorcas Worthington repre-
sented Tooele Stake. “Our president,
Sister Emily J. Anderson, could not come
she is a very efficient woman and wishes to
do all in her power to assist in every good
cause,” etc.
	Alvira L. Cox, president of South San-
pete Stake. “All our Stake has been visited
during the last three months, we have
talked about sending nurses and will try to
have some come and be trained. We have
had the grasshoppers this year, as you all
know, yet we have done well with our
grain in Manti. In Manti North Ward we
have a very fine building, we know what it
is to build with very little means ” Ex-
plained in detail the grasshopper ravages
and also spoke of the grain saving, lending,
etc.
	Sister Ida Smoot Dusenberry, counselor
in the General Society, addressed the meet-
ing for a short time, urged young women
to become acquainted with Relief Society
work. Spoke of their considering it an
ordeal to attend the meetings, thought the
day was far past that these meetings should
be just for old ladies. Talked of the
benefits of mothers’ classes, and felt if there
were any mothers not converted to the idea
they were the ones who should be called
upon and culled into the work. Knew
there was always time to dress twelve chil-
dren in the morning and get out to a circus
parade. Get the mothers out to these
mothers’ meetings, habit is a very great


[Column 1]
deal in many homes, it is comparatively
easy to do the things which have become a
habit. Made other appropriate remarks
and related incidents bearing upon this
subject.
	President Susan Grant, Davis Stake,
thought we should seek the inspiration of
the Holy Spirit, and try to be in touch
with all the branches in the respective
Stakes and see that they were doing their
duty in every respect; that was what she
was trying to do in her Stake, and to be
united with the Bishops and presiding
brethren. She felt they enjoyed the Spirit
of the Lord in their meetings and con-
ferences.
	Sister Emma A. Empey, superintendent
of the nurses’ classes, spoke upon that sub-
ject at some length. Said it required eight
months (the fee $10.00) to take the course
of training, and the extra course for obstet-
rics six months would be $40.00. Explained
the system thoroughly, told what good
work the nurses from Dr. Roberts previous
classes were doing, and that they were
sought after by the doctors because of their
skill and ability, etc. Gave an excellent
idea of the situation and the watch care
that would be over them while here in the
city engaged in their studies.
	Sister Phebe Y. Beatie, secretary and
treasurer of the nurses’ classes, spoke of
the privileges these young women would
have of attending the missionary course,
organ recitals in the Tabernacle, and other
things that would be educational. Alluded
to the opinion of the Bishops in the several
wards concerning these nurses, the great
blessing it was to have such help in times
of need, “they were like angels.” Said
those who had charge would see that these
sisters who come for this purpose had good
homes while here, Dr. Roberts would help
in this matter and they would have good
advice.
	Elder L. John Nuttall spoke a short
time in praise of the Relief Society work,
of Sister Smith conducting the conference
after the same plan as the General conference
had been in calling on presidents of Stakes to
report. Felt we needed more than one day
to hear from all and give instruction.
Wanted the sisters to go back to their
homes and take the counsel back with them
to others, and the spirit of the conference.
Brother Nuttall was anxious to render any
assistance possible to the society. He had
been among the sisters of the society in
Stakes and Wards, was interested in all
their work, wanted to see them advance
and blessed them in the name of the Lord.
	Pocatello Stake reported by Counselor
Matilda Smith, who told of the good work
being done there. Mothers’ meetings had
been organized and great interest manifested
in that and other lines of work.
	President Mary A. Sorenson gave a most
excellent report of Emery Stake, spoke in
high terms of praise of the late presi-
dent, Sister Annie U. Larsen, and felt she
would like to do as well as she had done,
thought many of the sisters in the confer-
ence would remember her. Gave some par-
ticulars about their grain and said she was
desirous of having more grain saved this
season. She hoped to be up and doing
with all associated with her, not found be-
hind in any good work.
	President Rebecca Standring, of Alpine
Stake, not being present, the secretary,
Sister Emmn Featherstone, reported. She
said: “We have mothers’ and daughters’

[Column 2]
meetings and they preside alternately, they
generally try to get a good lecturer to ad-
dress them and the meetings are well
attended and all go forth in their work with
joy and rejoicing.
	President George C. Parkinson, of Oneida
Stake, said he had enjoyed being present
and meeting the noble-souled sisters en-
gaged in this glorious work. Said there
are good sisters in Oneida Stake, they
travel around and do a great amount of
good in saving grain and helping in every
way, are always on hand to bless and com-
fort and are indeed a source of relief.
“Now, we have here our beloved Sister
Bathsheba, 80 years of age, she has been
faithful all the day long from the beginning
of this society, Sister Richards, nearly 80
and others; these good sisters’ labors
cannot be told in words. I endorse Brother
Nuttall when he said our good works will
show throughout the entire world; God is
the Author; mothers are the noblest souls,
providing tabernacles for the sons of men,
who will go forward and carry on this
work. It is your sons and your daughters
who will have the responsibility in the
future. Go to your homes with the spirit
of this mission upon you. God bless you;
God bless Sister Smith and lengthen out
her days with those associated with her in
this society and in the work of the Lord.
Amen.”
	President B. W. Smith said: “There are
a good many more we would like to hear
from, but the time is gone and we must
close our meeting.”
	Singing, Doxology. Prayer was offered
by President James E. Steele, of Bingham
Stake. Conference adjourned until next
April, 1903.
	E. B. WELLS, Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
	INTERNATIONAL DECLARATION.

	Last winter at the International Confer-
ence, a committee was appointed to draft a
Declaration of Principles which would
express the faith of the woman suffragists
throughout the world. This committee
was unable to complete the report, and
another committee was appointed, consist-
ing of the officers of the International Com-
mittee. The new committee was instructed
to draft a Declaration of Principles and to
submit them to the delegates to the Inter-
national Conference for approval. This
has now been done, and the Declaration of
Principles has been drawn up and signed.

	DECLARATION OF PRINCIPLES.

	We, the men and women assembled in
the First International Woman Suffrage
Conference, held in Washington, U. S. A.,
February 12-18, 1902, do hereby declare
our faith in the following principles:
	1. That men and women are born equally
free and independent members of the human
race: equally endowed with talents and
intelligence, and equally entitled to the free
exercise of their individual rights and
liberty.
	2. That the natural relation of the sexes
is that of interdependence and co-operation,
and that a repression of the rights of one
inevitably works injury to the other and to
the whole race.
	3. That in all lands, those laws, creeds
and customs which have tended to restrict
women to a position of dependence, to dis-
courage their mental training, to repress
the development of their natural gifts and

[Column 2]
to subordinate their individuality, have
been based upon false theories, and have
produced an artificial and unjust relation to
the sexes in modern society.
	4. That self-government in the home and
State should be the inalienable right of
every normal adult, and in consequence no
individual woman can “owe obedience’’ to
any individual man as prescribed by old
marriage forms, nor can women as a whole 
owe obedience to men as a whole, as pre-
scribed by modern governments.
	5. That the refusal to recognize women
as individual members of society, entitled
to the right of self-government, has resulted
in social, legal and economic injustice to
them, and has intensified the existing
economic disturbances throughout the
world.
	6. The governments which impose taxes
and laws upon their women citizens with-
out giving the right of consent, or dissent,
which is granted to men citizens, exercise a
tyranny inconsistent with just government.
	7. That the ballot is the only legal and
permanent means of defending the rights to
“life, liberty and pursuit of happiness”
pronounced inalienable by the American
Declaration of Independence, and accepted
as inalienable by all civilizations; therefore,
women should be vested with all rights and
privileges of electors in a representative
form of government.
	8. That the rapidly developing intellect
of women, resulting from new educational
opportunities, and the important position
in the economic world into which women
have been forced by the commercial changes
of the last half-century, call for the imme-
diate consideration of this problem by the
nations of the world.

SUSAN B. ANTHONY, Chairman, United
	States.
	VIDA GOLDSTEIN, Secretary, Australia.
	FLORENCE FENWICK MILLER, England.
	ANTONIE STOLLLE, Germany. 
	EMMA EVALD, Sweden.

A WOMAN MISSIONARY.

	[Mrs. Addison Pratt on the Society Islands
1850.]

(Continued.)

	The king is a very friendly man, agree-
able in conversation. Is pleased with the
American Elders because of their industri-
ous habits. He prides himself in doing
more work than other native man. He is
subject in his official capacity to the French
Governor.
	Today heard the news of the Tahitian
lady’s marriage to the king’s son, much to
the father’s displeasure. She had previous-
ly made an engagement with the captain
whose ship is lying here, and had received
many presents from him; now she re-
nounced him and chose a younger man.
I wish all white men who make contracts
with those women may be treated in the
same manner.
	Today the Pilgrim sailed. Mr. Rich-
mond called to make his adieux, proposed
sending from Tahiti any thing we needed,
was certainly polite, he may be sincere,
although given to flattery more than I ad-


[Column 1]
mire. He is recognized as a tolorably cor-
rect man contrasted with the fearfully disso-
lute. He has been a faithful friend to
Paraita.
	After the forenoon services we were in-
vited to go on board the vessel and drink a
cup of chocolate. We had a long walk,
crossed several deep streams. The men
stripped their feet and carried the women
over. The sea was rough. We ran the
little boat on the reef, reached the vessel
with difficulty, which was rolling so much
Sister Crosby and myself began to be sea-
sick at once, so we could eat nothing. We
concluded it was great labor and little
pleasure.
	I have at length got a new cook house
and everything arranged in good order,
shall be more comfortable. Again have
sent off letters to the United States to in-
form our friends that we still live and
breathe outside of the habitable world; gaze
at the same sun and moon and some of the
constellations, but not all. The north star
we cannot see, the grand cross is in the
firmament over us. To the same God we
pray and invoke blessings on them.
	There is talk of a vessel being fitted up
to take passengers to California. The chil-
dren are elated at the bare idea, uncertain
as it may be. Mr. Grouard thinks his
wife will not go. The dread of sea sickness
would almost induce me to remain here.
	The 27th. Attended the morning ser-
vice in the House of Prayer. After the
close Hoatan, our fatherly chief, addressed
the people on the subject of changing the
Sabbath day, which has for a length of
time been kept one day earlier than in
other parts of the world, owing to the
reckoning of the missionaries in coming
around the Cape. The people appeared in-
different, would not express an opinion.
The old chief spoke with warmth. He
said, “When the dances were introduced
you were ready to speak and give your
sanction; when spirituous liquors were
brought here to make the people drunk
you are ready to welcome its reception; but
when the subject relating to the worship of
God is being agitated you are silent.”
	They, at length aroused, set to thinking
and voted to have it changed.
	This morning I was angry and showed
my resentment. I received an unjust re-
buke it was impossible for me to feel calm
over. I repaired to the ‘Fere Burera” and
translated a chapter in Tahiti, by which
time my mind became composed.
	The natives seem bent on keeping two
Sabbaths together. They say if they
throw away the Sabbath they have been
keeping some of them will be thrown away
by death, such is their superstition. By
degrees they will be persuaded. 
The 29th. We ate roast pig for break-
fast, sent us by our good old chief. This
afternoon have received a large eel, two
feet in length; they are very fine eating.
The sea abounds with luxuries, and the
land teems with good things,
	The 30th. This morning very early

[Column 2]
went out to the reef in a canoe. It was
calm and beautiful sailing. We raised a
small sail and a gentle breeze wafted us
along smoothly. The water was clear and
we could see distinctly the coral beneath,
which resembles a garden of pines and
shrubbery, beautiful to look upon. The
air was cool, and light clouds shielded us
from the sun’s scorching rays. We sailed
in and out among the coral to examine the
fishing nets. They are curiously woven
out of nets sunk to the bottom where the
water is shallow, the bait put inside. The
fish run through the small aperture and are
secured. Sometimes fish weighing twenty
pounds are taken. We could discover them
of all shapes and colors shooting about and
hiding in the cavities of the coral. We
stopped on an island of rocks, struck a fire
with wood we carried out, roasted fish and
made a hearty meal.

(To be continued.)

Nos. 1-3-5 Main Street. .


Mrs. Alice M. Ruble, who has just been
nominated on the Democratic ticket for the
Colorado House of Representatives, has
lived in that State for thirty years. During
that time, it is said, she has made a host of
warm friends by her gracious, kind and
womanly bearing. She has held many
positions of honor and trust. She is secre-
tary of the Jane Jefferson Democratic Club,
has been secretary of the social science
department of the women’s club of Den-
ver, and has served on the board of directors
of the State Industrial School for Girls.
The scene was animated when Mrs. Ruble
was chosen in the Democratic  Convention
of Arapahoe county, Denver, and showed
how far it is from being true that women
receive less consideration when they have
votes. Eleven representatives were to be
nominated. Gen. John Charles Thompson
made a speech advocating the nomination
of women for two of the places, and urging
especially the choice of Mrs. Ruble. Miss
Edith McAtee, president of one of the
Democratic women’s clubs, recommended
that no woman should be placed upon the
ticket. “You have given the ladies 50 per
cent of the appointive offices, and that
should be enough,” she said. But the
convention was of a different opinion. Mrs.
Ruble, Mrs. Mary J. Wygant, Mrs Imo-
gene Golden Clark and Mrs. Harriet G. R
Wright were all nominated by their friends,
and so were a large number of men. After
the nominating speeches were all made,
Gen. John W. Browning moved that a
woman should be chosen before they began
to ballot on the men. State Senator John
A. Rush and Congressman Shafroth spoke
in support of the motion, and it was carried
without a dissenting voice. The conven-
tion proceeded to ballot on the four ladies.
Mrs. Ruble had 339 votes, Mrs. Wygant
168, Mrs. Wright 107 and Mrs. Clarke 99.
Mrs. Ruble’s nomination was then made
unanimous. It is interesting to note that
two Generals took the leading part in urg-
ing the nomination of a woman. It has
often been observed that military men are


apt to look with especial scorn on the argu-
ment that women ought not to be allowed
to have a voice in public affairs because
they do not fight.


ITEMS OF INTEREST.
	Dr. Hannah Graham has been appointed
one of the visiting physicians of St. Vin-
cent’s Infirmary, Indianapolis, Indiana.
	Dr. Alice Ellsworth, a recent graduate of
the medical department of Michigan Uni-
versity, has been appointed an interne in a
hospital in Minneapolis.
	Mrs. Ella Spencer Mussey, who has been
appointed the head of the women’s auxiliary,
committee at Washington for the coming
Grand Army Encampment, is the widow of
Gen. R. D. Mussey. She is the only
woman dean of a law school, and is said to
be a woman of much tact and executive
ability.
	In some places where there are no wells
of clear water, and the streams have to be
depended on, the water in the spring and
early summer is often so thick that it is un-
fit for use without cleansing. If prickly
pears or cactus can be obtained, twirl them
around in a barrel of water and it will soon
be clear. Some people use alum, but I
have not tried that, so cannot vouch for it.
Have a large barrel, put a pail of water in
it, break one egg in, then fill your barrel,
and in a short time it will be as clear as
crystal. The water should be changed
every day if used for drinking and cooking,
the barrel should be scrubbed and cleansed
out often, and rinsed out every day. B.R.
	Professor Jacobi, one of the most famous
physicians of Berlin, Gemany, has be-
queathed ten thousand marks to the Uni-
versity of Berlin, making it a condition
that the University shall not come into pos-
session of the money until women are ad-
mitted as regular students to at least two
departments. In regard to this gift the
Woman’s Medical Journal says: “A gener-
ous North Carolinan secured the admission
of women to the State University by a
large gift which he made on condition that
the University should be opened to women,
and Miss Mary Garrett, of Baltimore,
bought for women the right to study at the
medical school of Johns Hopkins, paying a
price for the privilege. Dr. Jacobi seems
to be the first German to take this practical
method of promoting equal privileges in
education.

THANKSGIVING.

Great Father kind, we thank Thee for light of sun,
The privilege to dwell upon this earth,
For everything which Thou for us hast done
Since through Thy mercy we were given births.
Ah, how can we our gratitude e’er show,
For all thy many blessings here below.

For sustenance of body, mind and heart, 
The bounties of the earth, the sky and sea,
This wondrous world, and all it doth impart—
Its light and shadow—all, all come from Thee!
Oh, may we learn to clearly understand
That even storms are mercies at Thy hand.

For music’s waves to ope the ice-bound caves
Of fettered thought—of hardened hearts—
To sanctify each grief, while spirit laves
Its bruised hopes to ease its wounds and smarts,
Sweet, soothing notes, let burdened spirits free—
Teach murmuring souls a true humility.

For wealth of learning’s lore we find in books,
For minds to ope our own unfolded thought,
For noble acts and gentle tones and looks.
Which Thou into each daily life hath brought.
Ah, gratitude snpreme indeed should blend
With every breath—to Thee, Unfailing Friend!

For earthly friends, and comforts, too, of home,
For kindred dear who bring us joys so sweet,
Who—though in distant lands we roam—
Make hope, and faith more perfect and complete.
Give pure incentive to each effort here,
Enhancing joys, e’en sweet’ning sorrow’s tear.

Ah then for love we thank thee more than all,
It beams through all the world’s dark wilderness.
Thy mercy heareth ev’ry mortal’s call—
Thy love which breathes the sweetest tenderness,
Which brings the tired pilgrims rest and peace,
Imprisoned spirits, too, a sweet release.

Makes light the darksome way with Living Fire,
Illumines every narrow, winding path,
Removes all doubt—awakens grand desire, 
Implants in human hearts Eternal Faith.
Oh! now, Dear Lord, unbounded gratitude
Accept for all these blessings pure and good.
						ELLIS R. SHIPP.


[Column 1]
BERTHA SPENCER’S HOLIDAY. 
	For weeks the Spencer household had
been in a state of excitement and prep-
aration, for the only daughter was going to
be married. The amount of work required
to get a girl “married off” properly is quite
astonishing, for with the shopping, the
sewing, the designing and fitting, the lists
for invitations, the house decorations and
the hundred other things that tend to make
a wedding, one’s mind and body too are
quite worn out, at least this was the case
in the Spencer family for Bertha was very
much beloved and everything possible had

[Column 2]
to be done for her by her doting parents.
But now the last week had arrived, the
pretty trousseau had been carefully ex-
amined and laid away with violet sachets
among the dresses, ribbons, gloves and
laces, and rose leaves and lavender among
the dainty lingerie and snowy linen, and
Bertha, like a school girl, was pleading
with her parents for one long, free day.
	“Just one day, mamma, all alone,” said
Bertha, “from one morning until the next,
to do just as I please and not to be dis-
turbed nor interfered with by any one.”
	So simple a request one might think
could be easily granted, but a popular girl 
engaged is seldom her own mistress. Soci-
ety, as well as a girl’s fiance always have
some claim and say on a girl’s time and
the manner of spending it. But throwing
care to the wind and standing on her own
ground Bertha persuaded her mother to
consent to her whim and grant her a day
of respite from all thoughts of the wedding
and from all thoughts of the future. She
wished to indulge herself just once again
in girlish fancies, and a sober-minded person
would say “pranks;” for well she felt, that no
matter how happy married life may be, few
are a woman’s own moments, when the re-
sponsiblity of a home and a man’s happi-
ness depend upon her.
	Two days before her marriage Bertha
claimed her day and positively refused to
see any one, even her mother. Bertha’s
home was just an ordinary, comfortable
home, such as as a man with a moderate
income and a woman with some tact and
management and a good deal of common
sense would provide; room enough, a gar-
den, an orchard, a pretty lawn and flower
beds, not too plenteous, but a pretty set-
ting to a cottage home where comfort
mingles slightly with a little artistic tem-
perament. And Bertha—well, she was
perhaps only an ordinary girl, too, neither
a beauty nor a genius, yet a pretty girl with
some accomplishments and brimming over
with sentiment.
	That latter trait easily shows why she
desired this one day all her own. The
night before she left her blinds partly
raised that the first faint rays of daylight
might enter her room and as they did she
awoke from her happy slumber and pre-
pared herself for the sweet day of idleness.
After dressing neatly in a pretty blue
gown, setting her room in order and offer-
ing her morning prayer she quietly let her-
self out of the house. No one was stirring
only the birds were beginning to awaken
and nature in its solitude was all the love-
lier. Down the garden walk, into the
orchard she ran. The dear old orchard,
how she loved its rows of lovely apple
trees, and currant bushes in between. Here
all her young life she had romped and
played, climbed the trees in fearless games
of back out, and swung out on the highest
limbs, then dropped into the dewy grass
beneath. In games of hide and seek she
had buried herself in the foliage of the cur-
rant bushes, and under the spreading shade
of the dear old apple trees had given many
theatrical performances. The forests of

[Column 3]
Arden had they been many a time, and
herself the lovely Rosaland, and here at last
—not in play, but in reality—the true
knight had come and won his own fair
lady.
	This glorious morning she threw aside
decorum and lived again for one brief hour
her happy childish days; along the old
familiar haunts she ran, climbed into the
trees, shook the branches, fragrant and
beautiful in their pink and white blossoms,
and as the snowy petals fell upon the
ground she laughingly recalled the day
when she entertained the neighboring chil-
dren, reciting “The Little Bare Foot;” and
to make the play more real had one of the
boys shaking apple blossoms over her in
imitation of a snow storm. Peeping into
the recesses of a large currant bush she was
almost shocked to see one of her own dear
dolls. Poor neglected little thing stained
and soiled with decaying leaves, tenderly
she raised it, her first bisque doll, ever so
small, but after all, the “prettiest doll in
the world,” notwithstanding larger flaxen-
haired, and gorgeously-dressed dolls had
later been her own.
	What a flood of recollections that little
doll brought back to her now, the differ-
ent dresses her childish fingers had fash-
ioned; how once she made a mourning dress
to go to a baby doll’s funeral. The mater-
ial was a piece of dear Aunt Louisa’s black
silk with purple stars, and Bertha, poor
thing, sewed it with white thread and
stitches to make a baby blush. Sewing
never was Bertha’s strong point, but she
was always good at preaching and on this
particular occasion she was elected parson.
In later life she never knew whether to laugh
or cry when she recalled that funeral sermon,
it was so touching in its ridiculousness, so
ridiculous in its touchingness. One other
reminiscence of the doll was a picnic on the
hills, where she with several other little
girls carried their dolls, dressed in gingham
(they were always most particular about
their dolls costumes) in a little express wagon
hired from one of the brothers and paid for
in apples. Here after the picnic they
gathered the wagon full of blue bells, sweet
peas and sego lilies. Sweet little doll, lie
there still in the leaves of the currant
bushes, for the day is growing. Let me
bury you here with the flood of recollections
of that sweet childhood.
	The sun was rising o’er the eastern hills
and glinting the leaves and dewy grass with
its brightness when Bertha descended from
her lofty fancies and realized that the sharp-
ness of the early morning exercises, had
made her hungry, accordingly she quietly
re-entered the house, ate with relish the
breakfast that the kind-hearted maid had
prepared for her, and taking along a dainty
lunch, again sauntered irito the garden be-
fore any of the rest of the household had
arisen Lolling in the grass in the shade
of the dear old garden, in girlish dreams,
the morning hours were spent.
	Is it time wasted in retrospect, if
we live a full measure of happiness,
sometimes in idle fancies and sweet
memories? Ah no! for time and places


[Column 1]
always change and the morrow, who can
tell? Even so with this sweet girl. The
garden of her home, her early playground
was destined soon to annihilation. The
beautiful trees, the graceful bushes the
waving corn all vanished, to make place for
great brick buildings perfectly expression-
less business blocks where never a familiar
thing recalls the graceful garden or the happy
home. Letting herself out the garden gate
Bertha sauntered along the street noting
nothing, simply breathing the fresh air and
enjoying to the utmost every minute of the
day. At last she reached the foothills and 
there lay the old graveyard where a tall
white marble slab marked the resting place
of a sweet, sweet sister. “My sister, my
sweet sister, if a sweeter name there were
it should be thine,” quoted Bertha, as she
seated herself beside the little mound. She
often visited here, for she loved and cher-
ished all the sweet associations of her early
life and as she thought of that lovely girl,
of how they had romped and played to-
gether, of how they had laughed and wept,
shared each others’ joys and sorrows, knelt
for years together in prayer, conned the
same books, practised the same lessons, the
tears came to her eyes and a longing to her
heart to whisper now the story of her love,
for she knew what sympathy and outpour-
ing of love that sister had for her. And
who can tell but that the whisperings of
her spirit were heard in that other world,
at any rate, her silent prayer gave her
great comfort, and a gentle calmness came
upon her and made her very happy as she
proceeded on her walk to the hills beyond.
Often before had she enjoyed the walks in
the canyon and among the hills, but usual-
ly in company with some gay companions
gathering wild flowers or autumn leaves,
and now almost from habit she gathered
the flowers as she saw them, but dropped
them just as soon, so her path was marked
with lovely flowers; on she walked, now
climbing the rugged rocks, again threading
her way among the maple and oak brush,
tripping occasionally in the tangled grasses
and catching her clothes in the thorns of
the wild rose bush. Like Marguerite she
counted the petals of the daisy, repeating
“He loves me, he loves me not,” and like
a mountain naiad she seemed when she
wove herself a wreath of wild roses and
fancied she was once again the May queen
with her long, glossy hair let down over
her shoulders that she might see the reflec-
tion of herself and better recall the school
days and the happy games of that yesterday.
She fairly laughed with the thought of the
game of Copenhagen when that dashing,
dark-eyed boy chased after her in and out
and under the knotted rope for the prize of
one kiss. The pink suffused her cheeks
even now as she recalled the thrill she felt
as his hand touched hers, clutched so tight-
ly on the rope. Poor boy, he too was gone,
the ocean furnished him a grave, and silent-
ly they who loved him suffered on, scarcely
realizing but that some day he would return.
With a flood of tender memories, she un-
consciously tossed a pebble in the stream,
and as the circles rose one after the other,
then broadened and broke into the rushing
water, so the rich recollections rushed
through her mind, making her oblivious to
all outward sounds, when suddenly she
was startled by the gruff, but pleasant
voice of a teamster, hauling timber from
the mountains, as he hailed her with the 
commonplace courtesy of the canyon road:
	“Have a ride, miss.”

[Column 2]
	Sitting on a rocky throne, with her
crown of flowers she looked like a Druid
queen, and the suddeness of the invitation
to ride startled her so that she seemed for
a moment quite bewildered and scarcely
realized herself the situation, untill the man
exclaimed:
	“By George, it’s a pity for so young and
pretty a girl as you to be daft.”
	She lightly laughed at this and accepted
the situation and the ride as well. It just
suited her mood to climb to the top of the
great logs so tightly chained together and
ride down the rough canon road. It was
a rude, jolting way she knew, for
she had tried it once before with a
school girl friend and the driver sang
them funny songs and told them funny
stories. Bertha enjoyed it then, but she
rather hoped this driver wouldn’t and her
wish was gratified, for thinking her mind
was affected, he was most careful how he
addressed her and seemed satisfied simply
to ask a few questions as to where she be-
longed and who she was, and Bertha per-
ceiving his mind concerning her, without
wishing to frighten him, still did not un-
deceive him, but allowed herself to wander
into fairyland again, and midst songs and
laughter asked him, “Had he met the fair-
ies in the hills, and did he see the queen
with her magic wand?”
	“Well, no miss,” he answered, “but
sure farther down the road I met a fairy,
and you’re it.”
	At which she laughed so gaily that he
felt more satisfied than ever of her insanity
and in order to keep her quiet, began to
tell of the beautiful mountain where the
logs were hewn, of the cold ice-like spring
and its tiny fall that is the beginning of the
canyon stream, where clinging to the rocks
along its banks are clusters of graceful
ferns and mosses, green and soft and rich
as velvet, and now and then long slender
leaves holding sprays of little white lilies
are found at the foot of the towering pines,
then high in the mountains, are the large
white and purple columbines.
	“Oh, I know,” she interrupted, “I found
some once when a jolly crowd of us were
picnicking near the upper mill; they are just
lovely.”
	“Say, miss,” said the teamster, “are you
crazy or just pretendin’?”
	At this question Bertha laughed so heart-
ily that he was more mystified than ever,
but she presently assured him of her
sanity and allayed his fears and false im-
pressions by telling him that she was just a
romantic girl taking a walk in the cañon
and indulging herself in fancies and idle-
ness “just for fun, you know.”
	After this explanation she jumped down
from the wagon, for by this time the
mouth of the cañon had been reached, and
thanking him for the “lift” began to pin up
her glossy hair, remove her flowers and
assume a young lady air once more.
	“Well I’ll be jiggered,” said the driver,
if you aint a fairy, you’re sure a picter. But
don’t get too romantic or some one ’ill be
running away with you for certain.”
	“Good bye,” laughed Bertha. “Thanks
again, I’ve enjoyed the ride so much.”
	’Twas dark when Bertha reached the
broad streets. The great hills behind
threw dark shadows across the cañon road
and the red glow of the setting sun still
lingered on the mountain’s snowy peaks
After one long loving glance up the narrow
cañon towards the old saw mill, with its
great wooden wheel standing like herself in

[Column 3]
picturesque idleness, Bertha hurried her
steps towards home, for she feared meeting
some friend or acquaintance and this lone,
idle day was not to be interrupted with any
ordinary social talk. Quietly she entered
the house and immediately sought her room;
locking herself in she indulged in a short
rest on her bed, for the long walk had tired
her somewhat. After a brief rest, Bertha
again sought the kitchen and the kind-
hearted cook whom she persuaded to allow
her to eat in the pantry and not tell the
family she had returned until she was again
safely in her own room.
	“Laws, miss, but I am glad to see you,
whatever notion’s this you’ve got, not to
see even your ma, or your young man,
who’ll sure be here pretty soon.”
	“Well, never mind, Polly, they all know
I’m all right, and mamma promised me
the day all alone and I’m going to have it,
so don’t let anybody disturb me tonight.”
	“All right, miss; but I do think, we’ll all
be pretty glad when your holiday, as you
call it, is over.”
	Again in her room Bertha spent the
early evening sorting ribbons and little
trinkets, she intended giving to a neighbor’s
little girl. With loving touch she brought
forth her last doll, such a beauty, then laid
it carefully away for some other little girl—
who knows in after years.
	The day had been ideal and now the
moon was just rising and its faint silver
light threw shadows in her room. Bertha
leaned out of her window still dreaming,
and thinking “This is the last leaf of my
girl-life book, can I look into the future
and see what awaits me there?”
	Her heart was lifted up with the intense
pleasure of her holiday, for she had indeed
enjoyed to the uttermost every moment,
had realized the poet’s thought,
 
	“There is a pleasure in the pathless woods,
	A comfort on the lonely shore,
	Society where none intrude by the deep sea,
	And music in its roar, 
	I love not man the less, but nature more.”

	Sweet girlhood, what a day that must
have been! And in after years ’mid life’s
stern realities how often would the sweet
memory of that day be wafted to her soul
by the delicate perfume of a flower, or the
tender note of some bird song. To a soul,
where sentiment lies deep, nature and retro-
spect always bring sweet thoughts of 

	“The tender grace of a day that is gone.”
				ANNIE WELLS CANNON.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


NOTES AND NEWS.
	Mrs. Anna Connor is county treasurer of
Shoshone Co., Idaho.
	Mrs. Carrie Chapman Catt was given a
reception during her recent visit to Denver
by Mr. and Mrs. W. T. Cornwall, on the
roof garden of the Cornwall flat. About
250 guests were present, and at the close of
Mrs. Catt’s address more than fifty new
members joined the Suffrage Association.
	It is a noteworthy fact that the Illinois
State Federation of Women’s Clubs at its
recent meeting endorsed with enthusiasm
the bill to give tax-paying women a vote
on all tax questions and for all tax officials.
A committee of influential club women will
work for the passage of the bill. We be-
lieve this is the first time that a State
Federation of Women’s Clubs, as a body,
has taken up the effort to secure any form
of suffrage beyond the school ballot.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


THE DEPTH OF LOVE.

Because he brought no tears to her dear grave,
	Many and many there were,
Who whispered when no single sign he gave,
	“He never cared for her”

But down within the silence of his soul
	A surging ocean swept;
Yet none could see the current onward roll,
	The tides that never slept.

Because I stand in silence when your eyes
	Look softly into mine;
Because no words to my poor lips arise,
	Because I give no sign;

There are, perchance, those who would dare to
		say
	There is no heart in me.
Beloved, let them cry! Be glad that they
	Can never sound our sea.
			CHARLES HANSON TOWNE.


THINGS THAT NEVER DIE.

The pure, the bright, the beautiful,
	That stirred our hearts in youth,
The impulses of wordless prayer,
	The dreams of love and truth;
The longings after something lost, 
	The spirit’s yearning cry, 
The striving after better hopes—
	These things can never die. 

The timid hand stretched forth to aid
	A brother in his need,
A kindly word in grief’s dark hour
	That proves a friend indeed;
The plea for mercy softly breathed,
	When justice threatens nigh,
The sorrow of a contrite heart—
	These things shall never die.

The memory of a clasping hand,
	The pressure of a kiss,
And all the trifles sweet and frail,
	That make up love’s first bliss;
If with a firm, unchanging faith,
	And holy trust and high,
Those hands have clasped, those lips have
			met—
	These things shall never die,

The cruel and the bitter word,
	That wounded as it fell;
The chilling want of sympathy
	We feel but never tell;
The hard repulse that chills the heart
	Whose hopes were bounding high,
In an unfading record kept—
	These things shall never die.

Let nothing pass, for every hand
	Must find some work to do;
Lose not a chance to waken love—
	Be firm and just and true,
So shall light that cannot fade
	Beam on thee from on high,
And angel voices say to thee—
		These things shall never die.
					CHARLES DICKENS.


[Column 1]
INTERESTING LETTERS FROM NAUVOO.
				NAUVOO, Nov. 6, 1902.
Mrs. Emma S. Woodruff:
	DEAR MOTHER:—This has been a most
interesting day for Asahel and myself. The
day has been sunny and beautiful. The
crysanthemums, some few roses and many
other beautiful flowers are still untouched
by the frost, lending their assistance in
making the landscape almost perfect. We
visited a great number of the former homes
of the Saints, including father’s, Uncle
Smoot’s and the lot where your house
stood, President Joseph Smith’s, President
Young’s, President Taylor’s, President
Snow’s Heber C. Kimball’s and many 
others. We also visited the Temple block,
where you used to take grandfather’s din-
ner to him when he was at work on the 
Temple. We walked through the lane
south of where you used to live, and fancied
we could see you tripping light-heartedly
along that same lane before the cares

[Column 2]
brought upon you through the persecu-
tions laid premature care and sorrow on
your young heart. I may be home in a
few days, and I may not; but when I come
I will tell you all about our visit. It must
have been a great trial for our people to leave
this beautiful country. It could be made a
veritable paradise with the labor our peo-
ple have expended in the dry west.
	I hope this will find you well, and that
God may bless you in your Relief Society
and Temple work and all things. With 
love to yourself and Alice.
	Affectionately Your Son,
		ABRAHAM O. WOODRUFF.


	NAUVOO, ILL., Nov. 6, 1902.
Mrs. Emma S. Woodruff:
	MY DEAR MOTHER:—Owen and I have
been enjoying ourselves immensely today,
visiting the beautiful city of Nauvoo.
Beautiful for situation indeed, but now sad-
ly neglected, as we wandered through the
streets and saw in numerous places the
well curb only, as a fence about the cavity
to keep cattle from falling in, as the only
evidence of what was doubtless once a hap-
py home, my heart felt sad to see this deso-
lation. Many of the old places are stand-
ing, however, but their grounds and sur-
roundings are sadly neglected indeed. The
site where once stood the magnificent Tem-
ple is now overgrown with weeds, where
the ground is not littered with boards, old
doors, etc., as it seems to be a sort of col-
lecting ground for junk. From stone taken
from the Temple a large school building
has been constructed, also parts of other
structures. A Catholic priest occupies the
house of Parley P. Pratt, we called on him
and were treated nicely. A large Catholic
school and convent stand across the street,
and the arsenal is joined to and forms a part 
of it. We visited the people living in
Uncle Smoot’s house and learned that a 
two room brick house had formerly occu-
pied the corner near by, which we supposed
was the one you lived in. The lot is now
nearly covered with a vineyard and one
lone peach tree, also a small piece right in
the corner is occupied with garden truck.
Bishop Hunter’s house over in the next
block is fairly well preserved. A clump of
three large evergreens stand at the west
end. Passing down the street from your
place southward we passed a three story
house belonging to David Yearsley, then
turning up a block we called at father’s
house which has been occupied for four-
teen years by a Mr. Rheinburger. The
place is in a fair state of preservation and
much the same as when he left it, except
that the fireplaces have all been closed up,
of which there was one in each of the seven
rooms. The porch has been removed
from the front and one built on the rear.
Two locust trees, one in the front and one
at the rear, appear to have been standing
guard for at least sixty years. The floors,
casings, stairs and other woodwork are as
of yore. The trap into the attic, the cleat
on the cellar door and other features of the
place show the characteristics of the build-
er. During the day we visited the homes of
Heber C. Kimball, Brigham Young, Lorenzo
Snow, Orson Hyde, Orson Pratt, Sidney
Rigdon, John Taylor, the Prophet Joseph, the
Mansion House, the Nauvoo House, and
many other places. The White house, the
oldest building in the place is still standing
and occupied. Wm. Law’s home has only

[Column 3]
the foundation left, also the mill owned by
him and his brother, Wilson. We strolled
along the beach in front of the Nauvoo
house and gathered some pretty pebbles,
some of them wonderfully marked Emma
Smith’s grave is located about two rods
south and one west of the Prophet’s home.
Just before sunset, having obtained per-
mission from the janitor, we mounted to
the deck of a large school house standing
on a commanding eminence and secured a
splendid view of the city stretched out be-
neath, the river in its splendid sweep
around three sides, Montrose across the
noble stream with the islands midway of
the same, and last but not least, one of the
grandest sunsets, it was ever my privilege
to behold. Nauvoo was well named the
peaceful, the beautiful. Surely our parents
sacrificed much for the Gospel. The Lord
bless you and comfort you.
				Affectionately,
						ASAHEL.


MRS. STANTONS LAST WORD’.
WOMEN APPEAL TO THE PRESIDENT. 
	In July, 1848, history records the first
movement among women for the discussion
of their political, religious and social
wrongs.
	Since then the demand for the right of
suffrage has extended over many countries
and has been granted, in one form or
another, in the United States, in England
and her colonies, in Australia, New Zea-
land, the Isle of Man and New South
Wales.
	Tax-paying women have voted by proxy
in several nations of the Old World for
many years.
	In the long history of woman’s wrongs
there never has been so favorable a time to
demand her complete emancipation in the
United States as now, for we have for the
first time in this Republic a president who
has declared himself in favor of woman’s
political equality.
	When President Roosevelt was governor
of New York he recommended the en-
franchisement of the women of the State in
his message to the New York legislature,
and expressed the same opinion on several
public occasions Now is the opportune
time for leading women to ask the president
to make the same demand in his coming
message to Congress for this act of justice
to thirty-six million American citizens how
defrauded of their most sacred right, one
that underlies all others, a voice in the
laws. For, as the fathers said long ago:
“No just government can be formed with-
out the consent of the governed.”
	In a speech made, by the President at
Fitchburg on Labor Day, he said that he
was “in favor of an amendment to the
Constitution of the United States, confer-
ring additional power upon the federal
government to deal with corporations.”
	To control and restrain giant monopolies
for the best interests of the people is of vast
mport, but of far vaster importance is the
establishment and protection of the rights
and liberties of one half the people of the
United States, the most moral half, too—
namely, women. 
	Surely there is.no greater monopoly than
that of all men denying to all women a
voice in the laws they are compelled to
obey.	ELIZABETH CADY STANTON.
	October 20, 1902.
					Woman’s Journal
