SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, DECEMBER 1 & 15, 1902


GENERAL RELIEF SOCIETY NOTICE.
	The Stake Secretaries of the Relief Soci-
ety in the several Stakes of Zion, also in
Missions and in branches of the Society not
included in Stakes or Missions, are hereby
notified to prepare complete annual reports,
statistical and financial, dating from January
1, 1902, to December 31, 1902, and forward
the same to the General Secretary, Temple-
ton Building, Salt Lake City, Utah, not
later than the middle of February, 1903.
	Wherever the Relief Society owns
buildings, halls or granaries, it is desirable
that should be made a separate item, and
how many in the Stake and in what wards
located; also any other important facts that
would be of interest to the General Officers.
			EMMELINE B. WELLS,
					General Secretary.


[column 1] 
	A CHRISTMAS THOUGHTS.

	All over the world, as it were, or through- 
out Christendom nowadays, the one festival 
of Christmas is religiously observed. It is 
in its custom a beautiful thing, teaching 
the highest lessons in so simple a manner 
that as the Savior said, "the wayfaring 
man, though a fool, need not err therein.”
	Charity, as set forth by St. Paul in his 
Epistle to the Corianthians, stands out con-
conspicuously on these occasions, and is 
fully exemplified in the lives of men, women 
and children, who have the Christmas 
spirit, with the Christmas giving, the cheer, 
the good will and unselfishness. Other-
wise no benefits accrue either to the giver 
or recipient, whether it be in word or deed. 
"Charity never faileth," how much is im-
plied in these three words. O, that we 
might not fail in the true heavenly charity 
ought to be the constant prayer of those 
who are striving to overcome. It is but a 
small thing to give of one's substance, but 
how great comparatively to receive the 
compensation of the soul that comes through 
reaction to the giver. The joy one feels 
but does not quite comprehend, which in-
variably comes afterwards to those whose
hearts are attuned to harmony and love for 
all mankind
	After all love is the great source of joy 
and of pleasure. "God is love" we are 
told in the scriptures, and the more men 
and women grow into His likeness the 
higher and purer will be the love they 
manifest or give expression to, however 
seemingly simple that development may 
appear. 
"He sendeth rain on the just and un-
just”—yet it is customary in the world to 
[column 2]
smile on one's friends and frown on one's 
enemies. This is not the lesson Christ 
taught. He said "Love your enemies." It 
is well at Christmas time to remember the 
Savior's words while rejoicing that He 
was born into the world. It matters not 
whether he was born in Bethlehem of Judea 
or in Nazareth, such disputations are idle. 
He was born into the world for the redemp-
tion of all mankind. He was the only per-
fect One, whose life and example it is for 
the professors of Christianity to follow, and 
yet so far do they depart from it, that the 
Bible tells us even the heathen who knows
not God, will receive greater blessings. 
The social observances of Christmas as 
family reunions are very enjoyable and tend 
to cement those together who are of one 
kindred or the renewal of closely inter-
woven friendships. It is true that in the 
hurry and worry of the present day we do 
not attend to the sweet amenities of life on 
which depends much of our happiness; we 
ignore and pass over them and go to great 
receptions and public excursions, parties, 
etc., and sometimes, yes often, neglect to 
cultivate our own. 
Much of this is from a mistaken idea of 
duty, but if persisted in would result disas-
trously. It is a good thing to be well ac-
quainted with one's own family and house-
hold, and at holiday times there is, even for 
the busy men and women a sort of respite, 
and if the mother's strength is not over- 
taxed with too much cooking it is the sea-
son for unalloyed happiness. 
It is significant of a better day when one 
hears on all sides reforms in eating; less 
meat or none at all, less pastry or none at 
all, coffee and tea abolished almost entirely, 
these things will simplify the home work; 
vegetables, fruits, cereals cooked in the 
plainest styles will tend to good digestion 
and thus make people happier without 
doubt. 
Our children nowadays eat too much 
candy, too many sweetmeats and by far too 
much meat. In this respect many who dis-
card tea and coffee disregard the Word of 
Wisdom. 
There does seem to be a tendency to-
wards reform in this direction, the Latter-
day Saints profess to believe in the Word 
of Wisdom and in the prolongation of hu-
man life, and yet many of them at any 
rate are very careless concerning these 
matters. However, as we are not writing a homily 
but giving a few thoughts on a matter now 
everywhere understood, it may be out of 
place to mention the Word of Wisdom or 
any other reform. Christmas should bring 
only "Peace and good will," and no sort of 
reproof even to those who indulge in ex-
cesses perhaps, this life is full of problems 
yet unsolved, and the Latter day Saints 
are trying to keep in advance of other peo-
ple in the higher code of morals, and there-
fore one naturally falls into the habit of 
introducing into conversation and composi-
tion some of the serious, everyday affairs or 
commonplace subjects. 
It would be wrong to destroy any of the 
good fellowship of the Christmas season by 
dwelling too much upon extravagances or 
excesses, but moderation in all things tends 
towards greater happiness and real enjoy-
ment than any extreme, even if it be an 
amusement. 
The Christmas spirit is one of forgive-
ness, it partakes of the charity that for-
gives seventy times seven, delighting in 
doing good to suffering humanity; it is like 
[column 3]
a mission of peace carrying a flag of truce to 
those who need it in all places, to the lowly 
and the weak; it brings an atmosphere with 
it of peace and of love, those who enter into
the real Christmas spirit seek to do what 
our Savior did when on earth. It is re-
corded of Him that "He went about doing 
good." What does that imply? Not giv-
ing costly presents to those who give in re-
turn, but in seeking to uplift one's fellow-
men, those who are poor in spirit as well 
as those who are in actual want of food and 
raiment. Let us speak kind words to 
those we meet, to the old friends whom we 
may have neglected, these are the little 
things that leave an influence, and are as 
much deeds of generosity as the giving of
one’s substance, for such acts flow from 
generous impulses and often leave a lasting 
impression, though one may not be con-
scious of the effect. It is comparatively 
easy to give of one's means and it may be 
more difficult to carry sunshine to those 
who are under a cloud, but the blessing 
comes to those who make some sacrifice of 
their selfishness, for selfishness is the root 
of evil. He or she who ministers to the 
wants of others is disciplining his spiritual 
powers is planting the seeds that will 
grow to bless and save. It is "casting 
bread upon the waters." "Good deeds 
double in the doing and the largest half 
comes back to the donor."


EDITORIAL NOTES.
	We beg the forbearance of our patrons
and subscribers for the prolonged delay in
issuing the Christmas number of the EX-
PONENT and trust we may be forgiven, as it
was for several reasons, too tedious to ex-
plain, utterly unavoidable.
	With reference to the book we expected
to get ready tor the holidays, entitled “Aunt
Em’s Stories of Long Ago,” we desire to
state that there has not been sufficient
money raised by subscription yet to pub-
lish; it will be necessary to get a larger
number of people interested in the publica-
tion before we can be assured of its success;
however we are still hopeful as to the re-
sult of the undertaking, and will in our
January number give an outline of what
the book will contain.


TESTIMONIAL TO SISTER M. I. HORNE.

		NOVEMBER 20, 1902.

	The anniversary of Sister M. Isabella
Horne’s eighty-fourth birthday was cele-
brated by the officers and members of the
Relief Society of Salt Lake Stake, in the
Barratt Hall of the Latter-day Saints’ Col-
lege, her first counselor, Sister Sarah Jenne
Cannon, presiding. Exercises opened with
the congregation singing, “Now let us re-
joice in the day of salvation.” Prayer
was offered by Dr. Elvira S. Barney. Sis-
ter Maggie C. Hull sang a solo written for
the occasion by L. L. Greene Richards, en-
titled “Our Mother.” The first speaker
was Sister Rachel R. Grant, who spoke as
follows:

	SISTER RACHEL R. GRANT.

	I am very pleased indeed to celebrate the
birthday of Sister Horne, and I feel grate-
ful that her life has been spared up to the
present time and for the work she has ac-
complished and the blessings she will re-
ceive hereafter for her faithfulness, and the


[column 1]
blessing which she has been to the Latter-
day Saints and for her counsel. I feel that 
we have much to be thankful for. That 
our lives have been spared up to the pres-
ent time to come forth at this day and re-
ceive the everlasting Gospel that has been 
revealed in this latter-day. We have so 
much to be thankful for as Latter-day 
Saints. As a people we are greatly blessed 
with the privilege we have to associate our-
selves together Of course the greatest trial 
I have is that I cannot hear, but I have so 
many blessings I cannot complain, but if 
we only will live so that we may receive
the instructions of God, there is nothing we 
are called to pass through but will be for 
our good and we can rejoice in it if we do 
our duty. I feel to ask the blessings of 
God upon Sister Horne that her life may still 
be spared and when she passes to the other 
side, she will meet her dear family and 
friends. I have been acquainted with her a 
great many years and am afraid we do not 
appreciate the lovely friend we have in her. 
My dear brethren and sisters, I think 
many times that we do not do enough as a 
people for one another in this life in order 
that we may rejoice and receive the bless 
ings we will have when we pass  behind the 
veil and the joy we will have in meeting 
loved ones that are there. I feel thankful 
and happy although I cannot hear what is 
said. Since I was a child, I always loved 
to read the Bible and I many times think 
how we live today as they lived in the days 
of the Savior, when we have inspired men 
with us and I know that the Lord has 
called them and it is for us as individuals to 
receive them in that way and manner. 
I pray that the blessings of the Lord may 
rest upon Sister Horne and the rest of the 
sisters. I think many times we are not so 
particular in regard to our associates as we 
should be. I do not wish to take up the 
time, but hope the blessings of the Lord 
will rest upon you all. I ask it in the 
name of Jesus. Amen. 
Sister Maggie C. Hull rendered a solo
and chorus, accompanied by a few of the 
Temple choir, "Only remembered for what 
we have done." 
	PRESIDENT ANGUS M. CANNON. 
	It has fallen to my lot to come in late 
and then to be called upon to address you 
for a few minutes. I will inquire if you 
can hear me? There is nothing more an-
noying than to see a person motioning with 
his mouth and not to hear any sound. I 
am very glad to be honored and to be priv-
ileged to meet with Sister Horne and the 
rest of the sisters and brethren too, and to 
congratulate her on her natal day. For 
eighty-four years she has toiled here on 
earth. I have been acquainted with her 
for fifty-eight years past and I knew her 
boys and girls, more particularly the boys, 
for the girls were not born until a later day. 
I thought while sitting here of the exper-
ience this woman has obtained in gaining a 
testimony of the Prophet Joseph Smith and 
in associating with the wives and brethren 
of some of the twelve apostles, for I be-
lieve she was acquainted with all of these. 
She was acquainted with the Prophet 
Joseph, and with his brother Hyrum, and 
with his sisters and his other brothers. 
She was acquainted with the families of the 
twelve apostles that existed in that day, 
before the martyrdom of the Prophet 
Joseph Smith. 
I have visited at her house many times be- 
[column 2]
fore I went away upon a mission. The visits 
that I paid at her house, associating with her 
family, her sons, her daughters and rela-
tives and herself, when she sat with regal 
dignity in the homes of her posterity, have 
been a green spot in my memory. When I 
have thought of a happy home and of the dig-
nity of a woman, a woman of gentle and 
queenly dignity, I have thought of Joseph 
Horne, Sister Horne, the children, and the 
government of their family and when I see 
her surrounded by the sisters here, I think of 
the responsibility of this Stake of Zion. 
When I look around and see the coun-
tenances of those who have been her co-
laborers and who have been her sisters in 
these associations, providing for the wants 
of the needy from the earliest of my recol-
lections of the Church. I wonder what the 
association will look like without these 
familiar faces, when so many of them have
passed away. 
	We are pleased to meet Sister Horne 
here, and when she goes hence she will find 
Patriarch Joseph Horne waiting patiently 
for her coming, and then when I think of 
the hopes and faith that have attended her 
well spent life in mortality upon the earth, 
I know their joy will know no bounds when 
they are reunited, for God has promised 
great blessings to those who receive Him 
and keep His commandments. 
	SISTER JANE S. RICHARDS. 
	I feel honored as I suppose you all do 
that we have the privilege of greeting Sis-
ter Horne on her birthday at such a late 
day in her life, that she looks so well and 
has her mind so good. I cannot see the 
lack of anything in her. I think it is such 
a great blessing that she has been preserved. 
I can remember Sister Horne about fifty-
nine years ago. I remember passing her 
one day. I did not know her name at that 
time. I was with my husband and I said 
to him, "Do you know who that beautiful 
woman is down there?" and he said, "I 
believe her name is Horne." Pretty soon I 
was introduced to her, and from that day to 
this I have always found a sister in her. 
My acquaintance with Sister Horne has 
been such that I have been closely asso-
ciated with her and learned many things 
from her. She was in the Relief Society 
when I was and I feel as though she has 
been a greater help to me than I could have 
been to myself. I never found her stern or 
indifferent or lack time to speak and be 
courteous, which I think is a great bless-
ing. I consider that she has been a blessed
woman. I have lived next door to her for 
many years, and in all that time I have 
never seen Sister Horne in a hurry or out 
of temper, but what I could approach her, 
and this is more than I can say of myself. 
I think to live happy is to be happy. I 
am thankful Sister Horne is with us today 
and hope she will live just as long as she 
desires and that we may be able to take her 
counsel as she gives it to us. May the 
Lord bless us all, is my prayer in the name 
of the Lord. Amen. 
	Recitation, Sister Rosannah Cannon 
Irvine, "The Monk's Magnificat." 
	SISTER BATHSHEBA W. SMITH. 
	My dear sisters and brethren, I am very 
happy to be with you on this occasion. I 
do not suppose I can make you hear, but I 
can tell you that I love Sister Horne and 
[column 3]
have taken great joy in her society and in 
her counsel. I know she has associated 
with some of the best people that were 
ever in this generation. She was acquainted 
with the Prophet Joseph, his wife, his 
mother and sisters and many of the good
Saints in Nauvoo, and I always consider 
she was one of the wisest and best women 
we have. We have received a great many 
good instructions from her. She has been 
blest with a good husband, which is anoth-
er good thing on her part. He raised 
another good family. I am proud to know 
her and to know that all love and respect 
her. We must not forget our dead, to live so 
that the Lord will bring them forth in the 
morning of the resurrection. I believe we 
can scarcely imagine the great joy we will 
have when we meet our dead friends and 
relatives, if we perform our duty here for 
them. We have a great labor in the Relief 
Society to perform, to comfort and cheer 
the poor and needy, the sick and afflicted. 
I pray the Lord to bless Sister Horne 
and all the sisters and brethren. I ask it in 
the name of the Lord. Amen. 
	Sister Martha H. Tingey, daughter of 
Sister Horne, spoke briefly in behalf of the 
family. 
SISTER MARTHA H. TINGEY. 
	Brethren and sisters, it is certainly most 
unexpected to me to be asked to speak a 
few words on this occasion. I hope that I 
shall be able to control my feelings suffici-
ently to express the feelings of my heart. I 
can say in behalf of the family that we ap-
preciate sincerely the loving kindness and 
the confidence and respect which has 
prompted this testimonial to our mother 
today. We feel that she is indeed blessed, 
and that she has been able, through the 
blessings of God, to obtain the greatest 
wealth that earth can give, that is the love 
and confidence of her associates, as is mani-
fested here this afternoon. Such wealth is 
inestimable, and it is the wealth that will 
go with her through all eternity. And I 
can assure you, my sisters, that this is the 
wealth that our mother has striven for all 
her life. And it is this principle that she 
has instilled into the minds of her children, 
that they are looking for something higher 
than this life, for something higher than 
this world. 
We appreciate and feel grateful to 
God, our Heavenly Father, that He has 
permitted us to be born upon this earth 
and to receive such good parents as we 
have had, and that He permitted them to 
live together so long to give us their coun-
sel, and to be united in instructing their 
children and leading them in the paths of 
righteousness. They had the privilege of 
living together as husband and wife for 
nearly sixty-two years, and I think I can 
say they were always united in the govern-
ment of their children, in instructing them 
in the paths of right. It was the desire of 
their hearts that their children should be-
come thorough Latter-day Saints. I believe 
I can say truthfully today that our hearts 
are set upon the things of God and we have 
an earnest desire to continue on in the good 
work for which they sacrificed so much. 
We thank you today, sisters, for this 
demonstration of loving kindness to our 
mother. I hope it will be an inspiration 
to her to go on in the good work and that 
she may continue to be worthy of the con-
fidence and respect of the Saints of God.


[column 1]
May this be our blessing, I ask in the name
of Jesus. Amen.

	SISTER ELMINA S. TAYLOR.

	My sisters I am sorry they have called
upon me to speak because my voice is so
poor. I am suffering from the effects of a
bad throat. If you cannot understand me
you will make allowance for it.
	I am proud today to be called upon to
make a few remarks in regard to our be-
loved Sister Horne  I have lived on the
same block with her for nearly forty years.
I have associated with her in her home and
in her labors among the sisters of Zion.  I
have served under her as a secretary for our
Relief Society in the 14th ward for many
years, and I have traveled through the
cities of Zion with her, and I can truthfully
say that I have never known a better
woman, one who is more full of the spirit
of inspiration and love of the Gospel and a
desire to do good on the earth while she
may live upon it. I can never remember
any circumstance where Sister Horne has
done anything that we should censure, but
has always been, to our knowledge, for the
advancement of the kingdom of God and of
the daughters of Zion. Her heart has been
filled with this and she has desired to per-
form the work of God in every way that
she could. We have enjoyed some of the
most lovely times in her home that we
have ever had anywhere. I presume be-
cause of that love and sympathy and the
spirit of the Gospel. She has been a model
for the daughters of Zion to take as an ex-
ample. And may God bless her, and may
her remaining days upon the earth be sweet
and peaceful, and may the love of God be
with her. May her children be preserved
and may they still continue to follow in the
paths of their parents is my prayer in the
name of Jesus. Amen.

	SISTER EMMELINE B. WELLS.

	I feel that this demonstration is more
than due to Sister Horne for the labors she
has performed. While sitting here I have
been thinking that if all the daughters of
Zion could have come here to look upon
her face and hear what she will say to us
afterwards, for no doubt she will respond
to what has been said, they would have
been well paid, for we never knew her to
fail on any occasion. I am sure she will
tell us something of the divine mission of
the prophet Joseph Smith. She is certainly
one of the first of our sisters who knew
him. I do not know if there are any older,
but I think not in this city. She has trav-
eled all over these Rocky mountains. She
came here in October, 1847, and has been a
valuable worker and a strength to the
women of Zion ever since. As you have
been told she was acquainted with the
Prophet Joseph Smith and his mother, who
was one of the greatest women that ever
graced this earth.”
	Sister Wells made other remarks concern-
ing veteran sisters; and in behalf of the sis- 
ters of Salt Lake Stake presented Sister
 Horne with an elegant fur cape and a
silk umbrella.

	The following is the substance of Presi-
dent M. Isabella Horne’s response and
address:
	Sister Horne was considerably overcome 
by the eulogies made and the kindness and
love manifested and the gifts presented, but
with her great self-control she rose to the

[column 2]
occasion and in a few well-chosen words
expressed her pleasure and appreciation of
the Testimonial and of the presents from
the Relief Society of the Salt Lake Stake,
thanking one and all of the sisters who had
shown her such honor and respect.
	Sister Horne spoke modestly of her efforts
made for the benefit of the Relief Society of
Salt Lake Stake, over which she had pre
sided twenty-five years within a few days,
and how large the Stake had grown during
that time, and even now when two other
Stakes had been taken from it there were
more than thirty wards. She spoke of her
great love for the sisters and her desire to
continue in the service as long as the Lord
was pleased to give her health and strength
to perform the duties of her calling; her
one desire was to serve the Lord and assist
in building up his work upon the earth  She
was thankful to testify of the truth of the
Gospel and the divine mission of Joseph
Smith the Prophet.
	Sister Horne had first heard the Gospel in
Toronto, Canada, where she was then re-
siding, and herself and husband were both
converted and baptized into the Church;
the Prophet visited Toronto and was the
guest of Brother and Sister Horne, who at
that early period had the opportunity of his
personal acquaintance, and from him learned
many precious truths,
	Sister Horne gave a glowing description
of the Prophet’s wonderful personality and
charming manner, the power that he had
and the heavenly influence that he carried
with him at all times; she related how she
entertained him again for several days after
the Saints were driven from Missouri while
she and her family were residing in Quincy,
Illinois, and from that time forward Brother
and Sister Horne were on intimate terms
with the Prophet and his family up to the
time of the martyrdom; they were also well
acquainted with Sister Lucy Smith, the
Prophet’s mother, a woman of wonderful
presence and prophetic power, greatly be-
loved for her many virtues and who pos-
sessed great faith and power in administer-
ing to the sick.
	Sister Horne has known all the Apostles
and veteran authorities of the Church from
the days of Kirtland. She refered to the
first organization of the Relief Society to
which she belonged and testified that it
was organized by the Prophet Joseph; also
alluded to President Brigham Young ap-
pointing her to take charge of the first Gen-
eral Retrenchment Association; she exhorted
the sisters to faithfulness and diligence and
blest them as mothers in Israel in the name
of the Lord
	The meeting was adjourned by singing a
selection by members of the Temple choir,
and the benediction was pronounced by
President Angus M. Cannon.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT


WOMAN’S EXPONENT


[Column 1]
IN MEMORIAM.
	EMMA BARBER PIKE.

Tread softly here, a dear one sleeps, 
	And angels guard the spot,
Their loving vigils ever keep, 
	And we forget thee not.
	Another friend and sister has gone to join
those in the silent land. We see her here
no more, nor hear her loving voice. Sister
Emma Barber Pike departed this life, Nov-
vember 5, 1902, and in one respect her wish
was fulfilled, as she died with the harness
on. She was faithful to the end and as
those who endure faithful to the end are
[Column 2]
they who will receive the reward of duty
done, her reward is certain.
Sister Pike was born in Birmingham,
England, January 20, 1839. When about
eight years of age she was baptized into the
Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints
by Brother George Baugh. At the age of
twenty she married Samuel Pike, a mem-
ber of the Church. About seven years
after their marriage they emigrated to Utah,
crossing the plains with an ox team. They
staid in Salt Lake for a short time, then
moved, to Coalville, where they lived for
six years; leaving there they next went to
Alma, Wyoming. Here Sister Pike was
called to act as president of the Relief
Society, which position she held for a num-
ber of years and made of the society what
she did of every work she set her hand to, a
success. Many are the good deeds she per-
formed, and many are the grateful hearts
who can truly say, “Her good works fol-
low her.” While living there they lost
their home by fire, and all they possessed
was burned in it. I have often heard her
tell of that disaster and what a grief it was
to lose what they had worked so hard to
get, but with her characteristic cheerfull-
ness and her unbounded faith and hope, she
went to work with a will to help to make
another home.
After leaving Alma they moved to Laketown
then to Bear Lake, where she was called to
act as president of the first Primary Asso-
ciation organized there, Sister E. R. Snow
organizing the association. She labored
faithfully in this calling while she remaind
there. After coming to Logan she was
called as president of the Sixth Ward Prim-
ary, in which capacity she labored many
years, even after being called as counselor
to the Primary of Ca[-]he Stake. In Octo-
ber 1901, she was made president of the
Cache Stake Primaries, which position she
held at the time of her death.
	When the Logan Temple was opened,
she was called there as a worker, and per-
formed her part of the work in that Holy
House for a number of years. In every
position to which she was called she was
faithful and true. Her whole heart was in
the work of the kingdom of God It was
her desire to do all that she could, and she
labored to inspire others with a love for the
work. She was a generous, whole-souled
woman, who feeling she had freely received
desired to give freely. I knew her best in
the Primary work, as we have labored to-
gether for many years, and I never knew
her to shirk a duty or to neglect one. She
had the love of all the children, and she
loved them. She often said she hoped she
would be permitted to labor with the chil-
dren when she passed behind the vail, as
to her there was nothing so holy and pure
as the love of a little child. She was not a
mother, but the instinct of motherhood
was shown in her deep love for the little
ones and her devotion to their welfare.
Nothing greater can be said of her than she
did all she could, and was faithful and true

True unto death to the work she was given 
	Faithfully labored to do all her part;
Earning salvation—a sweet rest in heaven;
Ever we'll miss thee, dear, faithful heart,
				MATTIE BLAIR HANSON.


President Roosevelt has sent to the Senate
a general international agreement in the
shape of a treaty for the suppression of the
traffic in girls for immoral purposes.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


TENTH ANNUAL CELEBRATED.

	The Reapers' Club met at the home of
Mrs. E. J. Stevenson, on the evening of
October 21, 1902, to celebrate the tenth
anniversary of their organization. Mrs
Maria W. Wilcox presided.
	The club was founded on October 3,
1892.
	Meeting was opened with prayer by Sister
Smith. Singing, "Now let us re-
joice."
	Elder Nephi L. Moiris then gave, by 
invitation, an excellent lecture on events
that transpired during the "Dark Ages.’’
The remainder of the program consisted
of a violin solo by Miss Georgie Young.
Recitation by Miss Frances Young, and a
dance, the minuet, by Miss Claire Wilcox.
A delicious luncheon was then served,
which was much enjoyed by all present and
afterwards a social time was participated
in. The members of the Utah Woman's
Press Club were our guests for the evening.
					JULIA A. DRUCE.


OBITUARY.

MARTHA SMITH BENNETT.

	It becomes our painful duty to record the death
of our beloved sister, Martha Smith Bennett. She
was born June 9, 1817, at New York, St. Law-
rence, Stockholm. She was the daughter of Asa-
hel and Elizabeth Shellinger Smith; was baptized
into the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day
Saints by her cousin, Hyrum Smith, and confirmed
by David Whitmer, was married to Hyrum Ball
Bennet, August 17. She was the mother of six
children, three of whom survive her, she was presi-
dent of the Meadow Ward Relief Society from its
first organization, it being in the year 1870, till 1897,
when she resigned feeling it too great a responsi-
bility in her old age, she passed to the great be-
yond, Oct. 12, 1902.

RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
Inasmuch as our Heavenly Father has deemed
it wise to summon from our midst to a nobler
sphere our beloved sister, Martha Smith Bennett,
ex-president of our Relief Society, whose wise and
motherly counsels have stimulated us to greater
determination of becoming more valiant in the
kingdom of our Heavenly Father, therefore be it
	Resolved, That we, her colaborers in the great
work of love mourn her loss as a mother and wise
counselor and a faithful worker in the cause of
truth.
	That we express our sincere sympathy for the
bereaved husband and family and pray our
Heavenly Father to let the comforting influence of
His holy spirit be with them in this, their hour of
trouble, that they may realize she has gone to en-
joy a rest prepared for the faithful and will come
forth to a glorious immortality and eternal life.
	That a copy of these resolutions be placed on
record, one sent to the bereaved family and one to
the Woman’s Exponent for publication.
ELIZABETH E. STEWART, Pres.,
EMMELINE BECKSTRAND, Sec.

IN LOVING REMEMBRANCE.

	Elizabeth Phipps Harper, born at Soham, Cam-
bridgeshire, England, in the year 1823. Became a
member of the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-
day Saints at Holborn, London, in 1856. Emi-
grated to Utah in 1868 with her husband and fam-
ily. Located at Pleasant Grove where she resided
until her death, Oct. 27, 1902. 
	She was the wife of Benjamin Harper, who pre-
ceded her only five days to the great beyond. They
leave a respected, family of three sons, one daugh-
ter, thirty-five grandchildren and two great grand-
children.
	Sister Harper was a faithful Saint, an active
worker in the Relief Society, filling the office of
teacher for the past thirty years and being a con-
stant subscriber to the WOMAN'S EXPONENT and its
firm advocate. She also acted as aid in the Prim-
ary Association for many years. The guidance
and training of the children was her constant 
theme, and as an expression of respect and esteem
for her untiring labors the Primary children of the
ward came in a body to her funeral and followed
her remains to their last resting place.

RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.

	Whereas God nr His infinite wisdom has seen fit

[Column 2]
to call home Sister Elizabeth P. Harper of the
Pleasant Grove Relief Society, be it
Resolved, That we deeply mourn her absence,
a faithful worker, a comforter in the hour of need,
a devoted wife and mother, and extend our sin-
cere Sympathy to the bereaved family in this their
hour of trial and admonish them to look to a divine
source for comfort and consolation. We regard
her many virtues as worthy of emulation.
Resolved, That a copy of these resolutions be
sent to the family; that a copy be sent to the
WOMAN’s EXPONENT; and that the same be placed
upon the records of the Pleasant Grove Relief
Society.
SARAH A. FOUTZ,
President
EMILY C. BULLOCK,
PAULINE E. BROWN,
Committee.

SISTER OLIVE STEELE.
 
	Sister Olive Steele Bywater, wife of James Bywater Jr.,
departed this lite at Corinne, Box Elder Co Utah, Dec.
1, 1902, after an illness of one week.
	Sister Olive was born in Logan, Cache Co., September
16, 1871; was the daughter ot Brother and Sister David
Steele and granddaughter of Brother and Sister Cyrus
Card, who brought her up as their own daughter, her
mother having died when she was one year old.
	On December 19, 1889, she was married to James By-
water, Jr. She was the mother of six children, two of
whom have preceded her to the spirit world.
	She was the secretary of the Woman's Suffrage Associa-
ation, and in March, 1899, was set apart as corresponding
secretary of the Stake Board of the Relief Society of Box
Elder Stake, and in that capacity filled her duties ac-
ceptably.
	She was a lovely woman and was highly esteemed by all
who knew her. The funeral services were held in the
Second Ward meeting house, Brigham City, and all who
spoke paid a high tribute to the character of the diseased.

RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.

	Whereas an Alwise Providence has seen fit to remov
from our midst and has taken to another sphere our be-
loved sister and colaborer, Sister Olive Steele Bywater,
be it
	Resolved, That we mourn her loss as a faithful and ener-
getic worker in the cause of truth.
	That we tender our sympathy to the bereaved husband
and children, and pray our Heavenly Father to bless and
comfort them with the influence of His Holy Spirit in
this their hour of trial.
	That a copy of these resolutions be placed on our rec-
ords, one be sent to the bereaved family and one to the
WOMAN'S EXPONENT for publication.
SUSANNAH P. BOOTH, Pres’t.
EMILIA D. MADSEN, 1st Vice Pres’t,
LOUISA E. PETERS, 2nd Vice Pres't,
ANNIE J. WIXOM, Secretary.
ANNIE H LITTLEWOOD, Ass't Sec’y.


R. S. REPORT. 

WOODRUFF STAKE.

	Minutes of Relief Society conference of
the Woodruff Stake held at Randolph Sep-
tember 14, 1902, President J. B. Muir pre-
siding. Meeting opened with the usual
exercises of singing and prayer. The
sacrament was administered by Brothers
William Muir and John Spencer.
	President Sarah Tyson, of the Randolph
Ward, reported the same in good condition
and had joy in her labors.
	Sister Hatch, of the Argyle Ward, said
their society was in fair running order, it
being such a small ward was a drawback,
and it was not fully organized on account
of the death of one counselor.
	Sister Call, of the Woodruff Ward Relief
Society, said they were in good running
order and all the members were on hand to
do their part.
	President Wall, of Lyman, reported the
society there as being very flourishing and
in excellent condition.
	President Hilton, of Rock Springs Ward,
reported that society in first class running
order, the sisters were working hard and
trying to do their duty.
	Sister Stevenson and Sister Badger,
visitors from Salt Lake City, with Stake
President J. M. Baxter and others of the
leading priesthood were present on the
stand.
Sister Stevenson, of Salt Lake City, said

[Column 3]
“I realize the magnitude of this great work,
we should all conie together with a feeling
to serve the Lord. The Relief Society was
organized by inspiration. We should be
mothers in Israel, the greatest and grandest
of all women.” Spoke of the Sunday
School, of the beautiful order therein, of
the importance of having good teachers for
our children. Said the prophets of old
looked down through the vista of time,
and longed to live in this day. We all
have a great and grand mission to perform
and the Church is to regenerate the world.
The Relief Society work is a self-denying
work and our boys should be cognizant
with it, keep it before our husbands and
sons and help them to see the magnitude of
the Relief Society work. Spoke of the love
and unity that should, be in the family
circle, teach our daughters how to be good,
wives and our sons to seek a wife by in-
spiration; said the duties of a Relief Society,
teacher were very sacred and they were the,
backbone of the Relief Society, asked the
Lord to bless the teachers and all that were
connected with it.
	Sister Harriet Badger was pleased to be
with the Relief Society sisters of Woodruff
Stake, felt weak in her position, but was.
trying to do her best; said she was one in
heart and handwith the Relief Society
work and it was good to take the meetings
to the aged and those who could not get out
to them. Spoke of the famine that was to
come, and said we must take care of our,
wheat. Read a portion of section 88 of the
Doctrine and Covenants. How we should
not find fault with one another, said there,
was so much to speak upon the Temple 
work, the redemption of the dead.  Bore a
faithful testimony.
	The choir sang ‘‘Awake, awake, put on
thy strength.”
	Professor Hague of the Church school of
Paris spoke in the interest of the same; said
he was here to represent and explain the
necessity of education in the Church
schools, it would be a great thing for the
Relief Society to take up. He believed that
of all the missions that of a mother in Israel,
was the greatest. ‘‘I believe every great
and good man owes to his mother his suc-
cess.” Gave some excellent instructions in
regard to the education of the young, said,
the Teacher of teachers descended below all
things that lie might arise above all things.
Every teacher in Zion must become ac-
quainted with grief and be a man of sorrow.
The ones who go astray are the ones to be
looked after. “Let us supplicate our Father,
‘What wilt thou have me do?’ Jesus, him-
self sought to know, his mission. ‘Intelli--
gence is the glory of God.’ We should
know the meaning of that great word Lat-
ter-day Saint; our bodies should be vessels
of holiness thus the prophet said, ‘How
beautiful are thy feet.’ I pray that the-
made.”
	The choir sang, “Heavenly Father, hum-
bly we beseech Thee.” Benediction by
Stake President J. M. Baxter.
	Evening session of Relief Society confer
ence. Called to order by President J. C.
Muir. Choir sang, “How pleased ancl
blest was I.” Opening prayer by Bishop
J. C. Greg, after which was sung ‘‘The
silver, gold and precious stones.” Minutes
read and accepted.
	Sister Stevenson from Salt Lake City
said example is better than precept, we
have met in conference again, it is a good


place to come; said she was a living monu-
ment of God’s mercy.  “I was once an in-
valid for many years I wished to die, but I
do not now. I can testify that we can
claim the blessings of the Lord if we live
for them. We want to instil into the
minds of our children this faith. My young
brethren and sisters should live pure lives,
how much better it is to begin right, we
know not when we may be called hence.
Let us not forget the dead or neglect the
wonderful work for them. If we have had
the opportunity of doing a work for the
dead and have not done it, how will we
meet them? If any of the brethren have
wives who do not belong to the Relief
Society, you know not what they lose, the
brethren should understand the importance
of the same. We are to be judged out of
the books, and I believe one of these is the
Relief Society books. We don’t want to
be at ease in Zion, we want to set at exam-
ple that our lives will be acceptable to the
Lord. Teach the young to be true and
faithful, teach them to be virtuous, guard
your daughters, guard your sons, each time
a daughter falls, a son falls also. May the
Lord bless you that you may not forget
your duty to yourselves.”
	A quartette by the choir, “Sweet Sab-
bath Eve,” was rendered. 
Professor Hague said, “I believe the
sentiments of the anthem, ‘Sweet Sabbath
Eve,’ are true. Do we not know that
breaking the Sabbath brings its penalty,
great men have recognized these laws we
cannot have our ease in Zion, we must
work and labor. He that lives for himself
alone lives for the meanest person on earth,
a half-hearted Latter-day Saint is a most
miserable object; let us serve the Lord and
we will have joy therein. Those who live
the lives of Latter-day Saints are the choice
ones, their lives are in accordance with
their testimony. May our Heavenly Fath-
er’s choicest blessings be with you all.
Amen.”
	Stake President J. M. Baxter felt to en-
dorse what had been said, and he wished to
urge upon the people to educate their chil-
dren in the Church schools. “May the
Lord bless us in our endeavors to do good,
and bless the sisters who have come to visit 
us.”
	Sister Alice Jacobson thought we had
had a splendid conference, was thankful to
those sisters who had come to visit us;
prayed the Lord to bless each and every
one.
	Sister Harriet Badger said she had en-
joyed herself very much; was thankful she
was privileged to bear testimony to the
truth of the Gospel. “I want to tell my
young sisters to help their mothers, as they
have a great deal to do. I ask the Lord to
bless us that we may be guarded by His
Spirit.”
	The choir sang the anthem, “Hark, the
Herald Angels Sing.” Benediction by
Brother G. A. Peart.
	KATIE H. EPPICH, Stake Sec.

[column 1]
		ANCIENT RELICS.

	One of the richest finds of relics of pre-
historic man is that unearthed by Captain
Francis Tuttle of the revenue cutter Bear
during her recent trip in the Arctic seas.
The investigations and discoveries were
made at the instance of Captain D F. Toz-
ler of the revenue cutter Grant, senior
officer of the revenue cutter service on the
coast. A house built by human beings
probably thousands of years ago was found,
and Captain Tolzer now has some of the
utensils used by the people who made it a
habitation.
	Two years ago a missionary at Point Bar-
row told Captain Tuttle of a remarkable dis-
covery the natives of the vicinity had made.
It was the framework of a house which
was partially embedded in a cliff fronting
on the ocean fifteen miles southwest of
Point Barrow. At the point where the
house was discovered protruding the cliff
rose sheer seventy feet from the ocean.
Forty feet above tidewater one end of the
house was exposed. The Indians first
noticed the end of the house nine years ago,
and year after year as the cl[-]ff crumbled in-
to the ocean the framework of the house
became more plainly visible. The house
was built somewhat after the plan of
structure of the primitive habitations of
America. The ribs of an enormous whale
or of some animal now extinct were used for
a framework, and over these, skins had
evidently been stretched. No trace of the
skins, however, could be found, but the
framework of the house was intact.

	PREHISTORIC UTENSILS.

	The utensils found at the spot consist of
a bailer for a boat, a carved ivory rowlock,
four stone knives, a bone knife and a skin-
ning knife. This skinning knife is a curi-
osity. It might be said to be in the shape
of a crude, old-fashioned drawing knife,
such as is used by carpenters, minus the
handles. It is about the length of a draw-
ing knife, but the stone blade, which is
fitted in the center, is about four inches in
breadth by three or four inches deep. The
boat bailer is made from the ivory of a
mastodon tusk. It is shaped somewhat
like an old-fashioned gourd dipper and will
hold about one pint of water. The bone
knife resembles a corn knife or machete
such as was used by the Cubans. A pecu-
liarity of this knife is an etching of a pre-
historic animal on the blade. The image
appears to be a rude caricature of a rhino-
ceros. There is a probability that the knife
may have been made from the bone of the
animal depicted on the blade.
	The ivory rowlock is also etched. The
work on this relic is much more delicately
and artistically done than that on the knife.
The etchings are of fish and sea monsters
and there are five or six of them.
	An effort will be made to bring the bones
of the house frame down from Alaska and
set them up in the Smithsonian institution
just as they were found in the cliff. The
fact that there were thirty feet of gravel

[column 2]
and earth above the house, and that all the
utensils were of stone and bone of masto-
don’s tusks, warrants the conclusion that
the house was built in remote antiquity.


NOTES AND NEWS.

	Mrs. Kipling, the mother of Rudyard
Kipling, has just published, with her
daughter, Mrs. Fleming, a little book of
poems entitled “Hand in Hand.”
	The Virginia House of Delegates has
passed by an overwhelming majority the
Cabell child-labor bill, to prevent the em-
ployment of children under twelve, in any
mill, factory or manufacturing establish-
ment, and to regulate the employment of
children between twelve and fourteen in
manufacturing establishments.
	At the election of December 2 and 9, the
following women, among others, were
elected members of school committees in
Massachusetts towns: Annie B. Bosworth,
Brockton; Mary E Mitchell, Cambridge;
Belle E. Curtis, Everett; Susan F. Flan-
ders, Melrose; Lizzie B. Blake, Woburn;
Annie M. Weeler, Haverhill; Clara T.
Guild, Medford; and Henrietta B. G. At-
wood, Somerville. The last three were re-
elections.— Woman's Journal
	Eleanora Duse, the Italian tragedienne,
was discussing woman suffrage not long
ago with a male friend. He said: “Man
was made first, you know, and woman
sprang from man afterward. She must be
his inferior.” Duse answered: “I cannot
agree with you. The flower comes after
the stem, but you surely do not call that
an evidence of inferiority.”—Ex.

POETRY—Hyrum Smith, Augusta J. Crocheron	57

	My Sister..........................L. L. G. Richards	59


HYRUM SMITH.
Hyrum ! Where’er the Prophet stood,
Thou, his other self wert there—
In all things sacred, high and good,
Or persecutions shafts to share,
Forgetting not the Love Divine
That ever near us watchful hears;
Thy brother’s heart still turned to thine
'Mid heavenly raptures or earth’s fears.

Beside him while the souls of men
The highest gifts of heaven received;
Beside him, hated, driven, when
At last by trait’rous friends deceived;
In Temple’s peace or home’s sweet bliss,
Or shackled in the prison’s gloom;
Thy life and love were bound in his
Sharing at last the martyr’s tomb.

Yet, from the stricken home there grew.
One worthy to bear thy great same;
Filling the years faithful and true
Until his highest calling came—
To lead God's people ! Ah, the tears 
The widowed mother suffering wept;
Her prayers, her vigils through the years—
In heaven’s blest treasury were kept !

The years may come and time may go.
But brightening as they swifter pass,
Ye martyred ones see here below
Your names and works grow grander, as
The Nations, self-wise in their race
Of pride and greatness, hurry on,
Forgetting in their worldly haste,
The holier victories to be won

Aye ! Thou art with him still, above
All hindrances, and thou dost stand
Serene, with him to watch in love
Thy people’s mighty mission, and
Mete blesssings to the pure,'
Defeat to all thy foes;
And watch, from Zion’s place secure, 
Heaven’s and earth's long drama close.
Augusta Joyce Crocheron.
S. L. City, Sep. 10. 1902.


[column 1]
	A FEW WORDS FROM JAPAN.
		TOKYO, JAPAN, Dec. 15, 1902.

Dear Folks at Home:

	It has been two weeks since I wrote to
any one except little notes, and today I
feel like writing again, for the mail that
leaves Yokohama tomorrow.
	This is such a beautiful, bright day. I
am sitting in my room upstairs with my
window open, and the sun is shining in
just like a warm spring day. The weather
is peculiar; we say, “How cold it is,” some
days, and sit around a big fire, wear over-
coats and furs when we go out, and still
we have had no freezing weather yet, and
no snow. On December 1st we had just

[column 2]
such a day as today, in fact we have many
of them, and others that are stormy and
cold. Yesterday (Sunday) it rained all
day and made me think of one of our cold
fall storms at home
	We have had three distinct shocks
of earthquake, too, during the last twenty-
four hours. We have these so often,
though, that we do not pay much attention
to them. They rock us a little in our beds
at night, and move the pictures on the wall,
or rattle the windows or dishes, but we are
getting used to this, if they are not worse,
we shall not mind it. I was going to say,
on December 1st I looked out into our
garden and saw one tree with bright red
autumn tints, another a pale yellow, one a
vivid, fresh, spring green, two or three
with barren branches, looking like the
trees at home at this time of the year, and
some full of buds that looked like they
were just ready to burst. Then there is a
whole hedge of camelias, the leaves a dark,
glistening green, and hundreds of buds
that come out a few at a time, and will con-
tinue to do so, I suppose, until spring,
whenever it is warm enough. We have a
bouquet on the table all the time of the
pretty pink and red flowers, the former
you can hardly tell from roses, and we still
continue to pick a few rosebuds from the
tree that grows by our window. Today as
I look out over the country I see a few of
the autumn tints, with every shade of green
for there is a regular forest of evergreens
and palms in our garden, and a great vari-
ety of trees whose foliage is strange to me.
In many places we see a little patch of
ground cultivated, with fresh green vege-
tables growing, looking like a spring gar-
den, but I am sorry to say that most of the
vegetables of that kind are new to us, and
that we do not like, and besides we never
use anything in this country that cannot
be washed and peeled, on account of the
way they use their fertilizers.
	I think this weather is beautiful, only I
feel like I had too many winter clothes on
today, but the boys are longing for winter
weather. When Thanksgiving and Christ-
mas draw near they want to see the snow.
	For fruit at this time of the year we have
oranges and apples, any amount of them.
The oranges are like those queer little
Japanese oranges that the children buy
down town sometimes, and a Chinese or-
ange that is more like the kind we have at
home, but not nearly so nice. We rather
like the Japanese oranges, they are very
juicy and peel very easily, the skin almost
comes off in one whole piece. but they are
very sour. and we have to eat them with
sugar. They are very cheap, though, one
day we bought three hundred for sixty-five
cents. The apples are very nice, I think
they are all imported.
	I must tell you about a visit we paid to
some of our Japanese friends not long ago.
Heber and I had been thinking for some
time that we should repay the calls of the
families where the boys are boarding, and
who have been so very kind to them and
to us. We walked to the first place, which
is only a short distance from here, and Miss

[column 3]
Mashimo and her mother met us at the
door, our shoes were removed and we were
given mats to sit on. The boys were all
there taking their lesson, so we did not
disturb them, but conversed with our
Japanese friends. Young Mr. Mashimo
came in and he can talk a little English,
and he assisted in our entertainment. They
first brought in a plate of cakes of various
kinds which they placed on the floor before
us. I tried to eat one, but it was very
peculiar, one of the kind Alma told about.
The inside was made of brown beans,
ground up with sugar, making a kind of
“gum-drop” substance, with dough on the
outside. We broke one in two, and Heber
swallowed his half, but I could not, though
I tried to emulate his brave example. Next
they brought in apples and the little Japan-
ese oranges. They do not consider it right
to pass you the fruit and let you wait on
yourself, but always peel it for you. The
fruit was quite nice, and we ate some of
that, also tasted some of the boiled sweet
potatoes that were served with the fruit,
but as we had just gotten up from the dinner
table at home before we started we were
not at all hungry. The next things they
brought in was some little crisp cakes that
looked and tasted like ginger snaps, but are
called “thunder cakes.” They have no
ovens to bake in, and these cakes are baked
in a kind of griddle, and from the noise
and sputtering they make while baking they
get their name. Mr. Mashimo gave us his
picture, and his sister gave us her thimble,
needle case and ivory marker that she was
using in her sewing, all of which are great
novelties to us. The thimble is like a
ring, and worn on the middle joint of the
finger. They are the greatest people to be
always giving presents, and we hardly know
what to do to keep even with them. We
harldly ever go to their houses that they
do not give us flowers and pins for the
hair, flower pots, fruit, etc. One young man
brought us a lacquer box full of sweet pota-
toes, mashed and all ready to eat, with his
very best compliments. When Brother
Grant was here before they gave him a
sword and offered him a pheasant, but he
could not accept that, as he could not pos-
sibly take care of it. Brother Ensign is
favored with birds. When he was here
alone living at a Japanese house he bought
a canary to keep him company, and one of
his callers seeing it thought he must be
fond of birds, and so gave him another.
Since we have been here Mr. Mashimo
brought him a skylark, a little girl gave
him another little bird of some kind, and
last of all the laundryman brought him two
more. He thinks of going into business
now.
	To go back to our visit. After spending
a very pleasant hour with the Mashimo
family, we said we would go to make our
call on the other family, where Sanford and
Fred are staying, the Nirayama family.
Mrs. Nirayama is the daughter of the
Mashimos. The names of the Japanese
nearly all have some signification, especi-
ally the given names of the girls. Niray-
ama means, the latter part, “yama” moun-


[Column 1]
tain, and the first part "good spirit." Mrs. 
Nirayama's first name is “Yuki,” snow,
and her sister's name, that is Miss Mashimo,
is "Yaye," cherry blossom. Speaking of
mountains, I have a fine view of"Fujiyama"
from my window, when it is pleasant, the
great volcanic mountain that the people
consider sacred, and that we see pictured on
everything almost, by way of decoration.
	In going to Nirayama's we had to take
rikishas, and Miss Mashimo ran out and
ordered them and her brother paid for
them before we knew anything about it or
could interfere. Arriving at the house,
Mr N. and his wife met us at the door and
welcomed us most hospitably. We had to
take off our shoes again here, and I decided
that if I lived in a Japanese house or visited
them very often, that I would certainly
wear Japanese shoes that can be put on and
off with little trouble, and are so much
like stilts that you never get your feet
muddy. 
	We had only been in the house a short
time when they invited us to go and see
the chrysanthemum exhibit. We were
pleased to go, and put on our shoes again
and laced them up ready for our short
walk.
	The gardens were beautiful with chry-
santhemums of every variety—pale laven-
der, pink, yellow, white, purple, and every
shade of red. The boys, Sanford and Fred,
had been there in the morning and had
bought a large plant for us. It was in a
large pot, and there were over twenty
beautiful crimson flowers on it; it cost
twenty-five cents for the pot and all, deliv-
ered. They had to fix a network of ropes
so that a man could carry it on his back,
and he walked about two miles to our
house. It would have been all the same if
it had been four miles, though, for as I
have said before, labor does not seem to
count for much in this land, where wash-
ing and ironing is done for two cents
apiece, no matter what the size of the 
article.
	We saw another plant that we admired
very much, and bought it for twenty-two
and a half cents There were about twenty
beautiful white flowers on it. When we 
got home our Japanese convert said we had
paid too much for it, about seventeen and a
half cents would have been about right.
How we all wished we could have had
some of these beautiful plants set down be-
fore you folks at home, I know you would
appreciate them as much as we do. We
have never been without flowers in our
rooms since we came here.
	After we had seen enough of the chry-
santhemum garden, we returned to the 
Niryama's and they insisted on our stay-
ing to supper. They will send out to a
restaurant and get foreign food for any of
us who visit them, but it is not cooked in a
way that is very appetizing, and we all
prefer their food, that is, some of it, and
what we don't like we don't need to eat.
Their fish is cooked very nicely, broiled
over a hibachi, sometimes, and then they
have boiled fish, pickled fish, sweet pota-
toes and lotus root sliced and fried in bat-
ter, beans cooked in sugar, and seaweed
pickled or boiled or something, I couldn't
tell you what, for it looked terrible, and I
could not get up sufficient courage to taste
it, although I tried to taste everything just
to see what it was. Their rice is cooked
very nicely, but without a particle of salt,
and you are not supposed to eat anything
on it, just eat it with chop-sticks. I am

[Column 2]
getting quite expert with chopsticks, and
the boys prefer them, for some things, to
knives and forks. They had lots of other
things, strange looking vegetables, pickled,
fried or boiled, and served with various
kinds of sauces, and soup with many
strange ingredients in it. I have counted
twenty different kinds of their food some
times when we have been invited out to
dinner. We sit on the floor to eat, and the
food is usually served on little trays, one
for each person, set on the floor before you,
but they have little tables sometimes, about
a half a foot or a foot high. We had a
terribly poor picture taken of one of our
dinner parties, which I will send you, so
that you can see the way we ate our dinner,
although we look anything but lovely.
	We had a very pleasant visit this evening
that I speak of at the Nirayama’s, spend-
ing the time looking at photographs and
curios that Mr. N. had brought from Ger-
many and other places. He is a very intel-
ligent man, has made a trip around the
world in a suite of a prince of the Imperial
household. He lived three years in Ger-
many, and visited all the principal countries
on his trip around the world. He has
some of the most beautiful decorations, pins
and medals that have been presented to 
him by the noted people whom he has met on
his travels. One we especially admired was
given him by Prince Henry of Germany,
whom we all heard so much about just
about a year ago. Mrs. Nirayama is a pret
ty, lively little person, and talks very fast,
and what we cannot understand from her
words she makes out with signs. She is
far from the downtrodden creature we are
led to suppose that all Japanese women
are, for she carries the money and the keys
and looks after the valuables and when her
husband wants anything he has to ask her
for it. We think it is wonderful and very
fortunate that the boys have been admitted
to such fine families
	After spending a very pleasant evening
we put on our shoes for the last time
and came home in rikishas, for which Mr. 
Nirayama insisted on paying. Heber in-
vited the families at both places and all the
boys to go to Asakusa Park next day to
see the temples, flowers, menagerie, etc.,
and we spent a very pleasant day there.

Monday, December 22, 1902.
	Two mails have gone since I commenced
my letter, but it has seemed as though I
could not find time to finish it Another
mail goes tomorrow, and I will make a
desperate effort to get my letter off by that.
We have been doing our Christmas shop-
ping for the folks here, and with lessons,
callers, etc., our time seems to fly. We are
making all preparations for a Christmas
just as near like what we have at home as
possible, and the boys have got a beautiful
tree and it stands in place in one corner of
our big dining room ready for the orna-
ments. Everything is so cheap here that
for a few cents each we have almost more
than we can put on the tree. We heard
over a week ago that our Christmas box
was coming from America, but we have
not heard of its arrival yet. (Since Aunt 
Gusta wrote this, I have received a card
that our Christmas box is in Yokohama.
H. J. G.) There is a steamer due today, and
if it does not come on that we will not get
it till after Christmas. We are all in hopes
it will come, for Mrs. Hedges said she has
sent a plum pudding for me and a box of
candy for Mrs. Ensign; and there is a cake

[Column 3]
for some one, besides the candy Lutie
speaks about, and we are all quite anxious.
The girls have been making candy today,
for fear it does not come. We have all
been invited to Yokohama for dinner
Christmas Eve, and to spend the evening at
Mrs. Bagnalls It was her daughter who
came over on the "Kaga Maru" with us,
she had been in America four years at
school, and had just graduated. They are
wealthy people, and Mrs. Bagnall has in-
vited us a number of times to call, but we
were quite surprised when we got such a
pressing and cordial invitation for all of
our little colony, everyone, underscored, to
come down to dinner Christmas Eve We
think it rather strange, and very nice, too,
that of all the people here our American
friends should single out the "Mormon 
Colony" for such a favor.
	The purser on the boat that Heber went
home on, the "Gaelic," remembered us
with a fine box of San Francisco candy,
and a little remembrance for Mary in the
shape of a candy plum pudding, on his last
trip from America.
	Heber has hired a rikisha man this last
month, so now we have our ' private car-
riage." and it does not cost so much as it does
at home Just think, he only pays seven dol-
lars and a half a month for the man, who
furnishes his own rikisha and boards him-
self. He has a wife and child to support
besides, and nothing but this seven dollars
and a half for everything they have to eat
and clothe themselves with. Of course
they don't have much clothing, she and the
child wear kimonos, and he wears some
little pants that do not reach to his knees,
and his feet and legs are bare this cold
weather. He wears some sort of a jacket
and a big hat. They have only one little
room, it looks about as large as our bath
room at home, and the rest of the building
he uses for his rikisha. It is built in the
front corner of our lot and from our stand-
point has the choice of location, that and
the woodshed, barn, etc., but that is the 
way they arrange things here.
	Christmas will be a thing of the past
when this letter reaches you, and I hope
you will all have a happy time. We are
all just as well and happy as we can be,
and I feel more contented here all the time.
Heber says all the time that we do not need
to think of going home for two or three
years longer, and I don't care so much as I 
did when we first came, then I thought a
year would be all I would want. I have
just room to sign my name, and send my
love to all of you.
With much love to you all,
Aunt Gusta.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


NOTES AND NEWS

Mrs. Squeirs, wife of the American min-
ster to Cuba, is trying to organize a society
for the prevention of cruelty to animals.
	King Edward’s coming to the throne has
been celebrated in India with gorgeous
magnificence. The report read like a chap-
ter from the Arabian Nights. The expense,
of course, has been lavish and enormous.
At the same time the newspaper dispatches
report a terrible famine in Finland—thou-
sand of brave and intelligent people are on
the verge of starvation—and in Russia a
frightful famine of still wider extent, en-
volving fifteen million hungry peasants and
untold thousands of starving horses and
cattle.                                       Ex.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT


SLUMBER LAND.

When stars gleam out in the evening skies,
	And flowers are hanging their heavy heads,
We journey off to a distant land,
	As the little ones rock in their cradle beds.

We glide through the twilight of Drowsy Lane,
	Where weary white eyelids drop softly down
And cover the light of the merry eyes
	That are blue and hazel and gray and brown.

We reach the valley of Half-Awake,
	Where the shadows ever so lightly fall,
And come to the dusk-veiled Bridge of Doze,
	Where giant Dreams are the warders tall.

And then, as the little heads nod and sway,
	And lower and lower sink slowly down,
We come to the wonderful Land of Sleep
	And enter its city, fair Slumber town.

The stars are the golden lamps that light
	The vision-paved streets of that city fair,
And lullabies are the music sweet
	That softly falls on the drowsy air.

And white winged dreamlets like angels float
	Above the spot where the children sleep,
And whisper them tales of wondrous things
	As they rock them off into slumber deep!
								SELECTED.


MY SISTER.

My Sister thou art troubled ! Wherefore move
So hardly in that old-time, narrow groove?
Get out of it! Step higher ! Then look back;
And see how graceless that low, misty track !
‘Tis but for earth-worms, mis'rable and cramped;
Our Father’s seal hath on thy brow been stamped;
Thou art His child ! Peacemaker thou shouldst be,
In every case which may appeal to thee,

And when unruly tongues would chafe thine ear,
With things unsisterly to speak or hear,
Which thou must answer, make thou this excuse,
“She is my sister whom thou wouldst traduce;
My Father's daughter ! And to soil her name,
Would give our family roll the mark of shame. 
Mistakes she may have made, but I must say 
Some pow’r misled her—it was not her way.”

Cease multiplying errors, black or red;
Erase them rather, leaving blanks instead;
Which she, our sister, may hereafter fill
With kindly deeds and words of sweet good will.
Should brilliant thought of thine another claim,
Be not too hasty to detract or blame;
For waves of thought like rays of light extend,
And who shall say where they begin or end ?

What matters it, since this last boon is free,
Of service in Our Father’s cause to be ?
If faithful as His daughter thou wouldst prove.
Climb on the ‘‘stumbling blocks,” leave the old
	groove,
Get higher ! Far above unholy strife,
Yet be more humble, in thy purer life,
For all who sland’rously their feelings ease,
Keep this kind motto’ “She's my sister, please.”

And when “the books” are opened at the last,
And the head Keeper over them shall cast
A just, though critical and searching glance,
When thy list shall be read, thou’lt hear per-
chance.
(As with “the other book” he shall compare,)
“Why were not credits entered here and there?”'
Wherein ’twas said, in place of speaking ill.
“She is my Fathers daughter—please be still.”

LOUISA L. GREENE RICHARDS.


THE NEW YEAR.

[Read at the New Year’s party, Cannonville,
Utah.]

	Good bye to the old year that has dis-
appeared for aye, never more to come
again. It has dealt kindly with us and we
will enshrine it gratefully in our memories.
	Few shadows lie on its receeding path-
way, and for the sunshine, the happiness
and pleasures of the past we may well be
thankful
	The hours that are past and gone we can
never recall, but in the future, as it comes,
every mistake can be corrected, and the
 hours be made to bubble over with useful-
ness and good.


The days of the new year will come
marching on, and may we improve the
shining moments that hare been designated
the “gold dust of time.”

New Year ! New Year dawning on us here,
Bring us many joys through this glad New Year,
May it hourly bring us peace and plenty too,
To the ones around us, though we number few.

New Year ! New Year ! may thy numerous days,
Glisten, ever glisten with joyous, happy rays;
May each flitting day a blessing to us be,
Until the last day of the year is but a memory.

New Year! New Year! may the months pass by,
Gliding smoothly for us in their coming nigh;
And our hearts beat happy till the welkin ring,
With another New Year’s praises, which we joy
	ous sing.

New Year! New Year ! we happy greet you here.
May we have no sadness, shed no bitter tear,
May our glad hearts raise in prayer, and praise
	and love,
To our loving Ruler in the heavens above,

EMILY B. SPENCER.


[Column One]
S. L. PRIMARY CONFERENCE.

The Salt Lake Primary held a most in-
teresting cenference in Barratt Hall, on
the 20th of December last. All the mem-
bers of the Stake Board were present on
the platform and they were most agreeably
surprised at the splendid turnout of the
little folks, for never before had there been
so well attended a conference. There were
830 children; besides the officers and par-
ents that were present, and the spirit and
enthusiasm of the conference were beauti-
ful and inspiring. Sister Camilla Cobb
presided in her customary genial and pleas-
ant way. The roll call was very entertain-
ing, each ward responding with a senti-
ment, after stating the number present.
A most interesting program was rendered,
each number being very much appreciated
by the little folks and deserving of special
mention, but two little girls, Rhea Little-
wood, of the 29th ward, and Lucile Snow
Critchlow, of the 8th ward, were cun-
niug in their recitations and the little
boys’ band, of the 8th ward, furnished
most pleasing music.
One of the features of the conference
was the sketch of the life of President Geo.
Q. Cannon, by his little granddaughter
Emmeline, and if at the conferences this
idea is carried out and the children give simi-
lar sketches of the lives of our beloved lead
ers, it will not only be instructive, but will
keep in mind their good deeds and their
devotion to the Church, and we take great
pleasure in publishing the sketch the little
girl gave with the thought that it will be
not only pleasant reading, but an example
for future use.                  A. W. C.

SKETCH
I live in the ward, of this Stake which
was named for my grandpa, George Q. Can-
non. He was one of the first to make his
home there. Long before that, my grand-
ma gathered thistles for food near the very
spot where our house now stands. He was
known by everybody in the ward and by
nearly everybody in this Church; and all
who knew him loved him, for he was
good all his days. He came to America
when a boy of fifteen years. He was born
seventy-five years ago in England. His
mother died on the ship and was buried at
sea  Soon afterwards, his father also died,
he was left at the head of a family of small
brothers and sisters. He met and loved
the Prophet Joseph Smith, though he was
only seventeen when that great man was

[Column 2]
murdered. He also knew, almost as if he
were a son, President Young and Presi-
dent Taylor. President Taylor was his
uncle, and with his family be came to this
valley the first year of its settlement.  He
was chosen first counselor to President
Taylor also to President Woodruff and
President Snow, Many years of his
life were spent on missions, and his
voice has been heard, not only in the coun-
tries of Europe and the United States, but
also in the lauguage of the islands of the
western sea. He was always obedient to
those placed over him and whatever he did
was by counsel. As a boy he made adobes
and hauled wood out of the canyons. As a
youth he dug gold, preached the Gospel
and wrote for the press. Between missions
he did emigration business for the Church,
and went to Washington to look alter the
welfare of his people. Then, as he grew
older, and after he became an Apostle, man-
aged newspapers, wrote books and for
magazines, sat in Congress and became a
leader in business throughout Zion. With
it all, he was ever a willing worker in the
cause of truth and one of the greatest
preachers and thinkers of our time. He
died on the 12th of April, a year and a
half ago. The great features of his life
were his humility, honesty and faith in
God. He knew and testified that the Lord
lived, and his highest, desire was to do His
will.


A WORD TO MOTHERS.
A child is the most helpless of all God’s
creation. The first thing for parents is to
look after the health of their children. In
doing so, dress them with warm flannel
next their bodies and have them eat at
regular hours, little meat, if any, and
plenty of milk and fruit
When old enough to run about they
should have plenty of outdoor exercise, in
cold weather as well as warm, carefully
dressed according to the weather.
Parents cannot bestow too much affection
upon their children. Children should never
be trifled with, but when speaking to them -
let them know that you mean what jou
say. Rule with love and kindness I
think a good plan for parents would-be to
learn to control themselves before trying to
control their children. It is wrong to pun-
ish in anger, it is better for children to obey
through love than fear, and to be taught
that they have a good Father in heaven
who sees them, and He watches over them
as well as their earthly parents.
Parents should teach their children the
principles of the Gospel, they will be held
responsible if they do not. They should
also teach them the object of their mission
on earth, how to control their passions and
appetites. Mothers should watch their
children very closely, find out their little
troubles and actions, encourage them to
ask questions and never fail to answer them
to their satisfaction.
Teach them the Word of Wisdom: that
it is their duty to prolong their lives upon
the earth. Children should be taught to
listen to the promptings of the Holy Spirit,
and to believe that it is their privilege to be
guided by it in all the affairs of life. Teach
them noble principles, that to be truly great
they must be truly good. Teach them to
be industrious early, in life and to exercise
every faculty they possess. Prayer in the
season thereof, also secret prayer.
MARY S. CLARK.
